==PAGE:1==
Properties of bricks produced from
Greenlandic marine sediments
Ida Maria Gieysztor Bertelsen, s114040
Master Thesis
Supervisors: Louise J. Belmonte, Wan Chen, and Lisbeth M. Ottosen
Department of Civil Engineering
Technical University of Denmark
01 February 2015
==PAGE:2==
ii
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis has the objective of investigating the possibility of a local brick production from ﬁne grained marine
sediments in the proximity of Sisimiut, Greenland. The assessment is based on the physical and mechanical
properties of clay bricks concerning the resistance to the harsh, Arctic weather conditions, together with an
identiﬁcation of a suitable production method.
Samples of ﬁne grained marine sediments were collected near the towns, Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq, on
the Greenlandic west coast, and tested with respect to the geological properties. The results revealed a ﬁne
grained, low plasticity, silty clay with evenly distributed grain sizes. A screening of the mineralogy (via X-ray
diffraction - XRD) and the chemical composition of the marine sediment samples was carried out to ensure ho-
mogeneity and investigate if the marine sediment occurrences had high contents of unwanted substances such
as sulphur, organic matter and salts. The results showed low contents of both sulphur and carbon, whereas
an unexpectedly high content of chlorine was found. This may cause problems such as surface efﬂorescence
on the ﬁred bricks and corrosion of the kiln during ﬁring. The XRD revealed clay minerals as illite, smectite,
and chlorite. The qualitative mineralogy was found to be very similar to those found in previous studies of
Greenlandic marine sediments.
After the preliminary investigations, a laboratory-scale study was made to determine the properties of ﬁred
brick pellets (d ∼20 mm, h ∼3 mm) with respect to shrinkage and water absorption, and of ﬁred miniature
bricks (∼54×54×70 mm3) with respect to compressive strength and water absorption. The results were com-
pared with ASTM requirements for building bricks to classify their resistance to damage by freezing.
Productions of brick pellets were carried out to investigate the inﬂuence of variables such as ﬁring temperature
(900 −1050 ◦C), ﬁring time (6 h, 3.5 days), forming pressure (20, 47 MPa), initial forming water content (10,
15 %) and content of granite waste (CR) (10 −20 %), in order to identify the most appropriate composition
and production procedure.
The results showed that the ﬁring temperature and forming pressure have a large inﬂuence on the durability
properties and that an addition of CR induces lower ﬁring temperatures and reduced ﬁring shrinkage and water
absorption. Moreover, no signiﬁcant difference in properties was observed for pellets ﬁred with a fast ﬁring-
cycle at DTU and with a slow ﬁring-cycle at Wienerberger.
Miniature bricks were formed with a pressure of 20 MPa and ﬁred at 1020 ◦C for 3.5 days at the brickwork
Wienerberger Tegl (Helsinge, Denmark). The study of miniature bricks showed that the water absorption was
too high after 24 h of submersion in cold water. It was concluded that the ﬁring temperature needs to be
increased in order to obtain a more durable brick-type, suitable for the Arctic climate.

ABSTRACT
==PAGE:5==
Resum´e
Dette kandidatspeciale har til form˚al at undersøge muligheden for en lokal produktion af mursten lavet af
ﬁnkornede marine sedimenter i nærheden af Sisimiut, Grønland. Vurderingen er foretaget ud fra de fysiske og
mekaniske egenskaber af mursten i forhold til at kunne modst˚a det arktiske klima. Desuden vurderes hvilken
produktionsmetode der vil egne sig til form˚alet.
Prøver af ﬁnkornede marine sedimenter blev hentet fra omr˚ader i nærheden af byerne Sisimiut og Kanger-
lussuaq p˚a den grønlandske vestkyst og blev testet for deres geologiske egenskaber. Resultatet var en ﬁnko-
rnet, siltet lertype med lav plasticitet og jævnt fordelte kornstørrelser. En undersøgelse af mineralogien (via
Røntgen-diffraktion) og de kemiske egenskaber af prøverne best ˜A¥ende af marine sedimenter, blev foretaget
for at sikre homogenitet og for at afgøre om forekomsterne havde et højt indhold af uønskede stof s˚asom svovl,
organisk material og salte. Resultaterne viste et lavt indhold af b˚ade svovl og karbon, men til gengæld et uven-
tet højt indhold af klorin. Dette kan medføre problemer s˚asom overﬂade-forvitring p˚a de brændte mursten og
korrosion af brændingsovnen i løbet af brændingen. Røntgen-diffraktionen viste forekomster af lermineralerne
illit, smectit og klorit. Kvalitativt viste mineralogien sig at være sammenlignelig med foreg˚aende studier af
grønlandske marine sedimenter.
Efter de indledende undersøgelser blev der udført en undersøgelse i laboratorie-skala for at bestemme egen-
skaberne af brændte murpiller (d ∼20 mm, h ∼3 mm) i forhold til svind og vandabsorptionsevne og af
brændte miniature-mursten (∼54×54×70 mm3) i forhold til trykstyrke og vandabsorptionsevne. Resultaterne
blev sammenlignet med ASTM-kravene for facade-mursten for at klassiﬁcere deres modstandsdygtighed over
for frost.
Der blev produceret murpiller for at kunne undersøge indvirkningen af parametre som brændingstemperatur
(900 −1050 ◦C), brændningstid (6 h, 3.5 days), formningstryk (20, 47 MPa), vandindhold før formning (10,
15 %) og indholdet af granitaffald (CR) (10 −20 %) for at kunne identiﬁcere den mest anvendelige sammen-
sætning og produktionsmetode.
Resultaterne viste at brændingstemperaturen og formningstrykket har stor indﬂydelse p˚a egenskaberne og at
tilføjelsen af granit (CR) medfører lavere brændningstemperaturer og mindre svind og vand-absorptionsevne.
Herudover viste der sig ikke at være nogen betydelig forskel p˚a egenskaberne for murpiller brændt ved en
kortvarig brændingsprocedure p˚a DTU og længerevarende procedure hos Wienerberger Tegl.
Miniature-mursten blev formet ved et tryk p˚a 20 MPa, brændt ved 1020 ◦C i 3.5 dage ved teglværket, Wiener-
berger Tegl (Helsinge). Undersøgelsen af miniature-mursten viste at vandabsorptionsevnen var for høj efter 24
timers nedsænkning i koldt vand. Det blev konkluderet at brændingstemperaturen skal hæves for at producere
en mere holdbar mursten som er egnet til det arktiske klima.

Preface
This study represents the Master Thesis composed by Ida Maria Gieysztor Bertelsen, at the Center for Arctic
Technology, Technical University of Denmark, DTU.
The thesis constitutes of 30 ECTS points, and was proceeded from the 1st of September 2014 to the 1st of
February 2015. The project was initiated with a ﬁeld work period in Sisimiut, Greenland, in August 2014.
The project was conducted with Associate professor MSK, Head of Section, Lisbeth M. Ottosen, PhD stu-
dent, Wan Chen, and Assistant Professor, Louise Joseﬁne Belmonte as supervisors.
Acknowledgements
I would like to thank my supervisors Louise J. Belmonte, Wan Chen, and Lisbeth M. Ottosen for their great
help, guidance, and inspiration throughout the whole project.
The assistance in the laboratory from Sabrina Madsen, Camilla Nielsen, Malene Grønvold, and Ebba Ceder-
berg Schnell from DTU BYG has been essential for the experimental work.
Furthermore, Klaus Myndal, Michael Ramskov, and Christian Rasmussen have all been very helpful with
designing the brick pressing form, the production and mechanical testing of the bricks.
Finally, I would like to thank Christian Kragh for his help with the layout of the report, proofreading, and
his support throughout the whole project.
signature
Ida Maria Gieysztor Bertelsen

Technical University of Denmark

1. Introduction
The increase in city population in the bigger towns in Greenland, and a critical housing situation due to many
dilapidated buildings, have resulted in long waiting lists on the housing marked (Kalaallit Nunaata Radioa,
2015; Grønlands Statistik, 2015). Since the majority of construction material used in the Greenlandic con-
struction sector is imported from all over the world, the costs are considerably higher than in e.g. Denmark
(C. and Jespersen, 2014). Based on this, the idea of using local resources, such as marine sediments in the
production of construction material occurred. The possibilities of using ﬁne grained marine sediments for a
production of building materials as masonry bricks, expanded clay products, and inert ﬁller material in concrete
or mortar, were investigated e.g. by Belmonte (2014). The conclusion was, that when using marine sediments
for production of building materials, masonry bricks are most suitable product. For a material to be appropriate
for a larger scale production of building material, aspects as accessibility, size of deposits, and characteristics
are of importance.
The overall aim of this thesis, is to produce ﬁred masonry units made of ﬁne grained marine sediments from
Sisimiut, Greenland, and to test their resistance to the severe weathering conditions in the Arctic region.
Figure 1.1: Sisimiut in the summer period
Furthermore, raw samples of ﬁne grained marine sediments from Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq, Greenland, were
characterized based on their geotechnical, chemical, and mineralogical properties.
The produced ﬁred bricks were classiﬁed based on requirements in accordance to ASTM-C62 (2013), in order
to determine whether the bricks are suitable as facing bricks in the harsh, Greenlandic climate.
The methodology of the work is described in Table 1.1.
1.1
Composition of the thesis
The composition of this thesis is illustrated below with a short description of each chapter.
Introduction
The purpose and importance of the project is stated in Chapter 1. Topics as the building tradition in Green-
land, the use of masonry bricks in Arctic regions, and the research methodology of the project are carried out.
Furthermore, a superﬁcial explanation of research of Greenlandic marine sediments made during the previous
years is illustrated.

==PAGE:14==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Theory
The theory chapters, Chapter 2 to Chapter 4, consist of a description the geological aspects related to the area,
and the formation of ﬁne grained marine sediments. The important properties for a material used in the pro-
duction of ﬁred masonry units in an Arctic region, and an out-line of the stages in the brick production are
described.
Materials and Methods
The chapters concerning the description of materials and methods are divided in two parts, one is a description
of the raw materials, and one is of the ﬁred units. Chapter 5 is a description of the raw material used in the pro-
duction of ﬁred units, and the laboratory work with respect to determination of the geotechnical, chemical, and
mineralogical properties. The investigated samples of ﬁne grained marine sediments, used as raw material in
the production of ﬁred specimen, were taken from different locations within two Greenlandic towns, Sisimiut
and Kangerlussuaq. Chapter 6 concerns the production and testing of ﬁred units with respect to the durability
properties such as water absorption and compressive strength, together with the ﬁring behaviour, which are of
high importance for bricks when used as facing building material in the harsh Arctic climate. The purpose for
each investigation method is described, followed by a summery of the experimental working procedure and the
materials used for the investigations.
Results
The results of all the experiments of are illustrated with graphs, ﬁgures, tables and photos, and shortly discussed
internally, but not compared with results from other studies. Chapter 8 concerns the results of the characteri-
zation of raw material, where Chapter 9 is related to the ﬁred units.
Discussion
In Chapter 10 and Chapter 11, the results from the characterization of marine sediments are discussed and
compared with previous studies of Greenlandic marine sediments. Regarding the testing of ﬁred units, differ-
ent production procedures and compositions of materials were investigated in order to observe their inﬂuence
on the durability properties. The results were discussed and compared to other studies of ﬁred ceramics.
Conclusion
In Chapter 12, the ﬁnal conclusions from the different investigations are clariﬁed and compared to the general
building situation in Greenland.
1.2
Building traditions in Greenland
The Greenlandic building tradition can be divided into two different periods; the Inuit building tradition and
the European building tradition implemented of the Danes. Until the 1950s the primary building material was
driftwood, stones, and peat (Vadstrup, S. and Schultz-Lorentzen, H., 1994), material that could be found in
local surroundings.
According to Grønlandskommissionens betænkning (1950) the houses were often small and poorly insulated,
which resulted in a bad indoor climate, why it was recommended to implement a new type of houses in Green-
land, which corresponded to the Danish standards. The building materials for the new types of houses were
brieﬂy discussed in Grønlandskommissionens betænkning (1950), and with respect to masonry construction, it
was concluded that it was inappropriate in the Arctic region, due to the low resistance to freezing-thawing cy-
cles of the mortar (Kalk- og Teglværksforeningen, 2014). This conclusion resulted in a townscape with houses
made of wood, concrete with facades of different materials, and only very few masonry houses.
==PAGE:15==
1.3. MASONRY BRICKS IN GENERAL AND AS BUILDING MATERIAL IN GREENLAND
3
(a) Traditional peathouse, Sisimiut, Greenland
(b) Wooden houses and the church, Sisimiut, Greenland
Figure 1.2: Traditional houses in Sisimiut, Greenland
Wood is an appropriate building material in the dry, Arctic climate, but have many disadvantages as low ﬁre-
resistance, high deterioration rate and thereby maintenance. Concrete is commonly not used as facing material,
but as inner bearing elements, why it is not exposed to the outer climate. Both materials need to be imported,
and because of the very isolated location of Greenland in the North Atlantic, import is expensive.
1.3
Masonry bricks in general and as building material in Greenland
Masonry bricks are generally considered to have many good qualities as a high durability, long service-life,
good thermal-, and sound insulating properties, high ﬁre-resistance, and great architectural possibilities as
colours and textures. Masonry is a composite material consisting of mortar and bricks, two materials with
different properties (Kalk- og Teglværksforeningen, 2014). Some of the problems related to using building
bricks as construction material in Greenland are
• Frost damage. When exposed to frost weather, porous bricks and mortar might be deteriorated due
to expansion of penetrated water, which, when freezing, is expanding (Kalk- og Teglværksforeningen,
2014).
• Construction procedure. With respect to the quality of masonry constructed during cold weather, some
of the most important procedures are to ensure that mortar maintain sufﬁcient heat for normal cement
hydration, otherwise it might slow down the development of compressive and bond strength (Brick
Industry Association, 2006a). This makes only the summer period suitable for constructing masonry
houses, if not any admixtures, that accelerate the hydration, are added to the mortar.
• Loss in experience. At the current time, no brickwork is located in Greenland, and the tradition for
building masonry houses is limited, why there presumably is a great lack of experience and educated
bricklayers.
Brick Industry Association (2006a) states that cold weather conditions, have some negative effects related to
the durability and quality of masonry, though adequate planning of the construction period and the choice of
material, will entail a durable masonry construction.

1.5
Previous studies of Greenlandic marine sediments and ﬁred units
Several studies have been made at DTU regarding investigations of ﬁne grained marine sediments, and the
usability of the raw material as construction material. The previous studies are listed in Table 1.2, where a
short description of each work is given.
It must be noted, that the raw materials used for production of ﬁred units in the present study, are not categorized
solely as clay based on the grain size distribution, why the notation ”ﬁne grained marine sediments” with the
abbreviation ”MS” is used as notation throughout this thesis (Belmonte, 2014).

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
==PAGE:19==
2. Geology related to brick clay
In the following chapters, background theory for the production of clay bricks is illustrated. The themes con-
cern a description of the geological issues of clay material, such as the quarternary geological history, together
with a description of the formation of ﬁne grained marine sediments, used as raw material in the present study.
Moreover, properties of importance for the raw material used as brick clay, is described in Chapter 3, on the
basis of the geotechnical, chemical, and mineralogical properties. At last, aspects and industrial procedures
related to the production of masonry bricks are clariﬁed in Chapter 4.
The raw material used in the production of bricks is commonly described with the notiﬁcation ”clay”, even
though a typical raw material in the brick industry consists of many different fractions of sedimentary-, or
residual rocks, with a grain size distribution which is not always categorized as clay. Furthermore, the term
”clay” has many deﬁnition in the literature, and may refer to both the particle size and the mineralogical com-
position of particles (Burns, 1979). In Section 2.1, the deﬁnition of the term clay is clariﬁed.
A short description of the geology in the area around Sisimiut is made in Section 2.2 together with an explana-
tion of the formation of the occurrences of ﬁne grained marine sediments in Section 2.3.
The raw material used for production of ﬁred masonry bricks in the present study is not classiﬁed as clay,
but as a silty clay loam, and will throughout the report be referred to as ﬁne grained marine sediments with the
abbreviation MS. When referring to the primary raw material used for masonry bricks in the theory sections,
the therm ”brick clay” will be used.
2.1
Deﬁnition of clay
The term ”clay” has many deﬁnitions in the literature, and according to (Guggenheim and Martin, 1995) it
was ﬁrst deﬁned and formalised in 1546, though it has since been revised many times. Today there are still
disagreements in the exact deﬁnition of clay, and the discussion concerns mainly whether clay is deﬁned by
the particle size or by the mineralogical composition of the particles (Burns, 1979). From a geological point of
view, clay is deﬁned as a sedimentary rock with particles in the < 2 µm fraction (Gribble and McLean, 2003;
Ovesen et al., 2012; Burns, 1979). The inappropriateness of this deﬁnition relates to the fact, that many non-
clay minerals occur in particle sizes within the clay fraction size of < 2 µm (Guggenheim and Martin, 1995).
Due to this observation, the deﬁnition of clay by the mineralogical composition is of importance, and concerns
the content of clay minerals, the so called phyllosilicates or layer-silicates, which are normally only found in
particle sizes < 2 µm (Burns, 1979; Grim, 1968; Moore and Reynolds, 1997). A more thorough explanation
of the nature of clay minerals is stated in Section 3.3.1. The ”non-clay” minerals in the clay fraction normally
concern quarts, amphibole, feldspar, calcite etc. (Burns, 1979; Holmboe, 2001).
2.2
Geology in the area of Sisimiut
Sisimiut is located on the Greenlandic west coast, about 67◦N and 53◦V, in an area with discontinuous per-
mafrost, with an average temperature around −4 ◦C (Foged, 1979; Belmonte, 2014). The town is located in the
zone between the Naqssugtoqidian orogen belt and the Archaic continent, and the bedrock in the area around
Sisimiut mainly consists of gneisses with bands of amphibole and intrusive granites (Henriksen, 2005). The
7
==PAGE:20==
8
CHAPTER 2. GEOLOGY RELATED TO BRICK CLAY
Naqssugtoqidian orogen belt arose in the collision zone of two parts of the bigger Archaic continent, has a
width of approximately 300 km, goes from Søndre Strømfjord in south to Disco Bugten in north on the Green-
landic West coast, and continues a cross the continent to the Ammassalik area in East Greenland (Henriksen,
2005). The sedimentary rocks found in Sisimiut, are presumably an outcome of eroded rocks of gneisses and
granite from the Archaic craton and the Naqssugtoqidian orogen belt.
During the last glacial period, Weichsel, from 117 −11.5 ka BP, the area around Sisimiut was ice-covered, and
deglaciated approximately 10 ka BP (Belmonte, 2014; Henriksen, 2005). After a glacial period, the melting-off
of glaciers and the Inland Ice caused an eustatic rise of the sea level, which induced that the ocean inundated
the littoral zone. Furthermore, the land masses, that through thousands of years had been compressed by the
load of the ice sheet, were exposed due to a glacial isostatic adjustment. The two processes were displaced,
since the rise of the land masses is a slow process, which takes many thousand years, whereas the rise of the
sea level followed the melt-off of the ice, and occurred much faster (Foged, 1979). As a result of this, the melt
water brought along sedimentary rocks, especially in the silt and clay fraction, which were deposited in the
marine environment in thick layers on the sea ﬂoor, why they are referred to as marine sediments. According
to Foged (1979), the land today has risen as a result of isostatic adjustment of the land masses, why the marine
deposits are exposed on the ground surface, though in many cases covered by deposits of sedimentary rocks in
the sand and gravel fraction. The highest deposits of ﬁne grained marine sediments are today found 110 −140
m above sea level.
2.3
Formation of ﬁne grained marine sediments
Sedimentary rocks are deﬁned as soil particles in different fractions, created as a result of weathering processes
of the bedrock on the surface of the earth. The process of the formation of ﬁne grained sediments, can be
divided into three steps, I) break down of the bedrock by mechanical or chemical weathering processes, II)
transportation of the break-off of the bedrock, which results in continuous break down of the particles, and III)
deposition of the sediments (Gribble and McLean, 2003).
The formation of ﬁne grained sediments in the Arctic regions is assessed to derive from erosion of the bed rock
by mechanical weathering processes as glacial- (ice), aeolian- (wind), and alluvial erosion (river) (Gribble and
McLean, 2003). Due to the low temperatures, chemical weathering processes are in a lesser extent the reason of
formation of ﬁne grained sediments. During the transportation, the sedimentary rocks are usually sorted after
their particle sizes, since larger particles need a higher velocity/force to be transported. (Henriksen, 2005). The
deﬁnition of particle sizes divided in fractions is shown in Table 2.1 (Ovesen et al., 2012).
Table 2.1: Particle sizes of sedimentary material (Ovesen et al., 2012)
Stones
Gravel
Sand
Silt
Clay
> 20 mm
2.0 −20 mm
0.06 −2.0 mm
0.06 −0.002 mm
< 0.002 mm
In the end of the last glacial period, Weichsel, the ice sheet retreated which resulted in large deposits of ﬁne
grained marine sediments in the deglaciated regions of the Greenladic west coast, as explained in the section
above. The sediments were deposited in the saline environment on the sea ﬂoor, and due to the rise of the
land, the deposits were exposed (Belmonte, 2014). Regarding the formation of clay minerals, the process is
highly dependent on the climate at the time when formed. In a warm, humid climate kaolinite is often the most
dominant clay minerals, whereas smectite tends to form in colder and drier areas (Worrall, 1975). The minerals
are hydrothermal alteration products of different types of silicate minerals as feldspar and pyroxene. Quarts is
a very resistant mineral, not susceptible to weathering. (Gribble and McLean, 2003).
==PAGE:21==
3. Properties of brick clay
For brick manufacturing, the raw material must have some speciﬁc properties to be considered suitable as a
brick clay, why the geotechnical, chemical and mineralogical properties which must be taken into account
are described in this chapter. According to Andersen et al. (1989); Madsen (1983); Holmboe (2001) some of
the most important properties for the raw material are related to the grain size distribution, plastic properties,
content of clay minerals and ﬂuxing agents. Some of the problematic elements are related to a extensive content
larger calcite grains, organic matter, and elements as chlorides, and sulphates.
3.1
Geotechnical properties of brick clay
Holmboe (2001) states that an even grain size distribution of the clay, silt and sand fraction is preferable,
due to the packing arrangement of the grains. A bimodal grain size distribution is unwanted, since it may
induce a higher porosity of the ﬁred product, why additional material is preferable to complete the grain size
distribution. A Danish brick clay has typically a content of 30 −60 % in the clay fraction, 25 −45 % in the
silt fraction, and 0 −45 % in the sand fraction (Holmboe, 2001).
The plasticity of a clay is related to the formability and its ability to absorb water. For forming methods such as
the soft- and stiff-mud process (Brick Industry Association, 2006b), the plasticity is of high importance, since,
when forming the bricks, a certain amount of water is be mixed into the clay to make it formable (plastic).
When the brick is formed, it must be able to hold its form during drying and ﬁring. The window where the
material has obtained plastic behaviours varies from clay to clay, and is deﬁned by the plastic-, and liquid limit
(Brick Industry Association, 2006b). The plasticity of a raw material is highly related to the amount of particles
in the clay fraction < 2µm, and thereby the content of speciﬁc clay minerals. According to Burns (1979) clay
minerals usually occur in particle sizes < 2µm.
3.2
Chemical properties of brick clay
The appearance and properties of bricks are highly dependent of the chemical composition of the clay. A
typical Danish red clay brick has the following chemical composition, SiO2 > Al2O3 > Fe2O3 > K2O >
CaO(+MgO), where the sum of the three primary oxides constitutes ∼90 % of the clay material (Andersen
et al., 1989). Regarding the colour of the bricks, the most signiﬁcant difference is the higher content of CaCO3
in yellow ﬁred bricks. The ratio of CaCO3/Fe2O3 > 3 gives yellow bricks, whereas a ratio < 3 results in
red bricks (Andersen et al., 1989). The development of haematite during ﬁring in red clay bricks is the reason
for the colour (Andersen et al., 1989). Furthermore, ﬂuxing agents as potassium, sodium, and partly calcium
oxide, and magnesium oxide must also be present to allow the clay to vitrify (Holmboe, 2001).
3.2.1
Problematic elements
Elements such as salts (chloride, nitrate, sulphate, and other sulphur compounds that might develop sulphate),
larger calcite grains > 0.25 mm, an extensive content of organic matter, and speciﬁc clay minerals in brick
clay might be lead to problems, when used in the brick industry (Madsen, 1983).
The general problem with soluble salts in masonry, is caused by crystallisation of the soluble salts, which
deteriorate the bricks because of the pore pressure. When ﬁred masonry bricks which contain an extensive
9
==PAGE:22==
10
CHAPTER 3. PROPERTIES OF BRICK CLAY
concentration of salts are saturated, it may lead to efﬂorescence and a growth of crystals on the surface. The
extent highly depends of the type of salt. The most common situation is, that salts are introduced to the brick
from the surrounding environment, though it can also exist inside the raw material in the initial phase. Some
of the most common salts related to masonry bricks are chlorides and sulphates (Hewat, 1996; Teknologisk
institut, 2010).
Chlorides are most frequently present as sodium chloride, NaCl, or calcium chloride, CaCl2 6H2O. Chlorides
are easy soluble, why they can easily be transported inside the porous brick. A high concentration of chloride
can result in efﬂorescence on the surface, which is not aesthetically nice. Furthermore, chlorides will evaporate
when ﬁred, why a high content has the possibility to damage the ﬁring kiln by causing corrosion (Madsen,
1983; Teknologisk institut, 2010). According to Madsen (1983) a raw material with a chloride content higher
than > 0.1 wt% is commonly considered unsuitable for brick production.
Sulphates are most frequently present as calcium sulphate (gypsum), CaSO4 2H2O, or Sodium sulphate,
NaSO4 xH2O. A content of sulphates, often in the appearance of gypsum, might cause discolourations and
efﬂorescence on the surface, and possibly deterioration due to crystalline pressure. Sulphates > 0.5 wt% is
considered to be inappropriate for brick production (Madsen, 1983). Sulphates are less soluble than chlorides,
why more water is needed before any negative effect is appearing, though they are more damaging when in-
troduced to the internal pores. Furthermore, other sulphur compounds as iron pyrite, can during ﬁring change
into sulphates, and a high content is therefore as well considered inappropriate. If higher contents of e.g. chlo-
rides or sulphates are present in the brick clay, barium carbonate, BaCO3, can be added to the raw material to
neutralise the unwanted effect of the salts, though it is not preferable, since it complicates the manufacturing
and is expensive (Madsen, 1983). Carbonate minerals as calcite grains, CaCO3, with particle sizes bigger than
0.25 mm and a content > 0.5 wt%, are considered as problematic, since the CaCO3 will, during ﬁring, change
to CaO, also known as quicklime. Quicklime has the possibility to expand when dry slaked, why hydration
of the nodules of lime can cause sufﬁcient volumetric expansion inside the brick which results in cracks. The
problem is known as ”lime blowing” (Madsen, 1983; British Geological Survey, 2007).
As it will be mentioned in Section 3.3.1 about clay minerals, the forming procedure of moulded bricks ne-
cessitates a high plasticity clay (Brick Industry Association, 2006b). The clay mineral smectite, is the main
reason for the clay to behave plastic, though a too high content of smectite results in a high ﬁring shrinkage.
The mineral has the capacity of obtaining high amount of water in between each T-O-T-layer, which evaporates
during ﬁring, and makes the brick shrink. The same is valid for a clay with a high content of organic matter,
since it can contribute to a greater plasticity, however an extensive amount of organic matter leads to a higher
shrinkage (Mezencevova et al., 2012).
3.3
Mineralogical composition of brick clay
The essential mineralogical content of a brick clay, is minerals and quarts, although other minerals such as e.g.
feldspar, calcite, and mica, also may occur in varying quantities (British Geological Survey, 2007; Andersen
et al., 1989). The clay minerals, such as e.g. illite, kaolinite, smectite, experience a continuously structural
breakdown during ﬁring, and are essential in brick clay, since they are the cause of the sintering of the material
during ﬁring, and thereby act like the ”glue” in the brick (Holmboe, 2001). Quarts and feldspar are both
non-plastic minerals, and do not break down within the ﬁring temperatures the typical brick is ﬁred at. These
minerals are important in brick clay as an internal structural framework to prevent exaggerated deformation
and shrinkage of the bricks during drying and ﬁring (British Geological Survey, 2007; Andersen et al., 1989).
3.3.1
Clay mineralogy
As stated, clay minerals are of importance in brick clay, since they act like the glue in the ﬁred brick product.
They are formed in soils and sediments, and are a hydrothermal alteration product of rocks, especially feldspars,
==PAGE:23==
3.3. MINERALOGICAL COMPOSITION OF BRICK CLAY
11
Figure 3.1: Structure of the clay minerals illite, smectite, and chlorite (Schweizerische Geotechnische Kom-
mission, 2015)
but also dark-coloured minerals as olivine, augite, and hornblende are relatively susceptible to hydrothermal
alteration (Gribble and McLean, 2003). Light-coloured minerals as quarts and muscovite are resistant to the
weathering processes and do therefore not form clay minerals. Water is essential for clay mineral formation.
The most common clay minerals are kaolinite, illite, smectite, vermiculite, and chlorite, and are normally only
found in particle sizes < 2 µm and called phyllosilicates or layer-silicates (Holmboe, 2001; Andersen et al.,
1989).
All clay minerals are composed of planes of cations, and constructed from two types of sheets, a tetrahedral
sheet, and an octahedral sheet (Grim, 1968; Burns, 1979). A traditional clay mineral incorporate both the
octahedral sheet and one or more tetrahedral sheets (Burns, 1979). A Tetrahedral sheet (T), is corner-linked
sheets of oxygen, O−, and silicon, Si4+ (or less frequently ferric, Fe3+, or aluminum, Al3+). An Octahedral
sheet (O), which is edge-linked sheets of hydrooxyls, OH−, and magnesium, Mg3+, aluminum, Al2+, ferric
Fe3+.
The classiﬁcations and the properties of the layer-silicates clay minerals, are based on the features of layer type
(1:1 or 2:1), and whether the octahedral sheet is of dioctahedral or trioctahedral character.
The clay minerals found by Belmonte (2014) in samples of ﬁne grained marine sediments (MS) from Illulisat
on the Greenlandic West coast are illite, smectice and chlorite, why the structure properties of these clay
minerals will be explained below.
Illite is a 2:1 layer group, consisting of one octahedral layer (O) (gibbsite) in between two tetrahedral (T)
layers, T-O-T, with poorly hydrated potassium (K) ions in between. The interlayer potassium cations prevent
the entrance of H2O into the structure (Tulane University, 2015; Grim, 1968), why illite is a non-expandable
mineral and a member of the mica group (Moore and Reynolds, 1997).
Illite is the alteration product of acid, igneous rocks as orthoclase feldspar (Gribble and McLean, 2003) with a
considerably amount of potassium. Under the weathering of the rocks, the potassium must remain after break
down of the remaining minerals (Grim, 1968). The dehydroxylation of illite occurs at 700−900 ◦C (Holmboe,
2001).
Smectite is a 2:1 layer group, T-O-T, with exchangeable cations H2O in between. Smectite is a highly expand-
able mineral, and has the ability to absorb large amount of water in between each T-O-T layer, which causes
the structure to expand (in one direction), and is the reason for smectite obtaining the typical plastic behaviour
of clay. A small amount of smectite clay is preferable, since it helps the non-swelling materials behave more
plastic, so forming of bricks by moulding into intricate shapes is possible. An extensive content of smectite is
undesireable, since it leads to a high shrinkage and deformation of the bricks during drying and ﬁring due to
the high content of water (Clay Minerals Society, 2015). Smectite is an alteration product of both light-, and
dark-coloured minerals as feldspar, and biotite, containing a considerably amount of magnesium (Grim, 1968;
Gribble and McLean, 2003). The dehydroxylation of smectite occurs at 700 −900 ◦C (Holmboe, 2001).
Chlorite is a 2:1 layer group, T-O-T, with a brucite layer in between. It is related to the micas, and is an
alteration product of minerals as biotite, pyroxene, and hornblende (Gribble and McLean, 2003). The dehy-
droxylation of chlorite begins at 550 ◦C (Grim, 1968).
==PAGE:24==
12
CHAPTER 3. PROPERTIES OF BRICK CLAY
3.4
Addition material
Recycling of wastes as additional raw material in ceramic building material has been done successfully in many
countries, where waste materials as stone-cutting was investigated by Belmonte (2014); Torres et al. (2009);
Menezes et al. (2005); Acchar et al. (2006); Vieira et al. (2004); Segadaes et al. (2005); Acchar et al. (2006) with
respect to the physical and mechanical properties of ﬁred ceramic units, in order to ﬁnd whether a waste type is
suitable. As it will be explained in Section 5.2, an addition of granite waste (throughout the present report, the
abbreviation ”CR” is used for granite waste) is included in the production of bricks in the present study, since
Belmonte (2014) stated that an addition of CR would lower the ﬁring shrinkage of the bricks. Belmonte (2014);
Torres et al. (2009); Menezes et al. (2005); Acchar et al. (2006); Vieira et al. (2004); Segadaes et al. (2005);
Acchar et al. (2006). concluded that an addition of granite waste in ceramic bricks resulted in high quality
and durable bricks, even though a worsening of the durability properties as water absorption, bulk density, and
compressive strength was observed in some of the studies. Granite is a non-plastic igneous rock, with feldspar,
quartz and mica as major components (Segadaes et al., 2005), and like the great majority of traditional ceramic
products, their major constituents, expressed as oxides, are silica (SiO2) and alumina (Al2O3), followed by
lime (CaO) and alkaline oxides (Na2O, K2O) (Segadaes et al., 2005).
3.5
Mechanical deterioration of bricks in an Arctic climate
The main reason for deterioration of ﬁred masonry bricks when used in a cold environment is the freezing-
thawing cycles. When a porous material, e.g. masonry bricks, absorbs water, and the temperature suddenly
drops, the water inside the pores crystallizes, which causes a pore pressure. If the pore pressure is too high it
might cause damage of the bricks ASTM-C62 (2013).
3.6
Knowledge about marine sediments from previous studies
Some of the previous studies related to marine sediments from Greenland, are shown in Table 1.2, and the main
conclusions are listed below.
• Samples of MS, distributed over a large geographical area, have very similar grain size distribution,
major element chemistry, and qualitative mineralogy (Belmonte, 2014).
• All the investigated samples have low plasticity indexes, which might lead to problems when forming the
bricks (Belmonte, 2014; Larsen et al., 2001; Sørensen and Welinder, 2003; Olsen and Johansen, 2013).
• A high concentration of chlorines was encountered in MS samples from Kangerlussuaq, and Ilulialik
(Jacobsen et al., 2002; Sørensen and Welinder, 2003). A low concentration was observed in samples
from Sisimiut (Foged, 1979; Olsen and Johansen, 2013).
• Clay minerals such as illite, smectite, chlorite (and kaolinite) were encountered in small amount in MS
from different geographical regions along the Greenlandic west coast (Belmonte, 2014; Alm and Brix,
2005; Jacobsen et al., 2002; Morelli and Bennedsen, 2005; Foged, 1979).
• It is possible to produce masonry bricks with ﬁne grained marine sediments as primary raw material. MS
from Illulisat and Sisimiut were found to have the most suitable properties (Belmonte, 2014)
==PAGE:25==
4. Production of clay bricks
Masonry bricks are produced by ﬁring of clay units, and are characterized by their prismatic shape. A masonry
construction consist of masonry bricks laid in different patterns with a thin layer of mortar in between, which
constitute a durable structure (Lars German Hagsten and M. P. Nielsen, 2005). This chapter, treats the typical
production procedure of masonry bricks at a brickwork. The procedure from the excavation of raw material
to ﬁring of shaped clay units are described. The different production methods are varying a lot and depend on
the speciﬁc properties of each brick clay. The production of bricks is resource and energy demanding due to
the ﬁring at high temperatures, and long drying time of the material. According to Brick Industry Association
(2006b) several brick manufacturers attempt to make the brick production more sustainability by locating the
plants close to the sources of raw clay material so transportation is reduced, by recycling of different types of
waste, and by taking measures to reduce plant emissions. According to Brick Industry Association (2006b),
most brick clay deposits used, are located within 500 miles of a brickwork.
Due to this statement, it is obvious that the raw material used in a production of ﬁred units in the present
study must be located close to a town. In Greenland there are no roads between the different towns, why all
transportation is conducted by ship. To make the project as realistic as possible, the samples collected for the
investigations were collected in the area of close proximity of Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq.
4.1
Excavation and preliminary processing
Every brick production begins with localisation of deposits of a suitable raw material together with a charac-
terisation of the soil, since the ﬁnal brick product depends to a great extent of the type of raw material. The
preliminary events before the bricks can be formed are I) excavation of raw material used as brick clay, II)
transportation to the brickwork, III) storing, and IV) preparation of the clay by pugging and rolling to break up
larger clay clods, stones and potential lime clods (Krejbjerg et al., 1991; Brick Industry Association, 2006b).
Usually the the particle size of the material is controlled and if the the grain size distribution of the raw material
is considered inappropriate, additional material as sand, silt, clay, or different types of waste is mixed with the
original raw material (Brick Industry Association, 2006b; Holmboe, 2001).
4.2
Forming of bricks
The forming method depends on the plastic properties of the brick (Holmboe, 2001; Brick Industry Association,
2006b) and typically covers three different methods.
The soft-mud process (moulding) is normally used for clays containing 20 −30 % of water to become plastic.
Is normally too wet to be extruded by the stiff-mud process.
stiff-mud process (mechanical extrusion) is normally used for clays containing 10 −15 % of water to become
plastic.
dry-press process is normally used for clays with a very low plasticity. The material is mixed with up to 10
% of water, and pressed under forming pressures of 3.4 −10.3 MPa in a hydraulic press. For investigations of
ﬁred units in other studies, higher forming pressures have been used by e.g. Gencel et al. (2013); Romero et al.
(2008); Acchar et al. (2006); Faria et al. (2012); Torres et al. (2009).
F´odi (2011); Chen et al. (2011) concluded that the forming method has a high inﬂuence on the physical and
mechanical properties, such as higher water absorption and linear drying-, and ﬁring shrinkage, and compres-
13
==PAGE:26==
14
CHAPTER 4. PRODUCTION OF CLAY BRICKS
sive strength, of the ﬁred product. The same is valid for the forming water content, where an increase in water
mixed with the raw material before forming, leads to a worsening of the durability Mezencevova et al. (2012);
Chen et al. (2011).
In the brickwork, Wienerberger Tegl in Helsinge, Denmark, where the miniature bricks in the present study are
ﬁred, the bricks are formed by soft-mud process (moulding), and stiff-mud process (mechanical extrusion), 80
% and 20 %, respectively.
4.3
Drying process
The water contents of the formed bricks, mainly depend on the forming method, and thereby the plastic- and
liquid limit. Typically the water content of the bricks is between 7 −30 % (Brick Industry Association, 2006b)
and brought down to 2 −3 % during drying (Køster and Hansen, 2014). The reason for drying the bricks is to
make the free water evaporate. The drying rate is typically controlled and adjusted to the speciﬁc material. If
the outer part of the brick dries/shrinks faster than the core, it might result in a formation of tension cracks in
the brick (Holmboe, 2001; Mezencevova et al., 2012). Typically the drying time and drying heat is 24 −48 h
and 40 −200 ◦C (Brick Industry Association, 2006b).
4.4
Firing process
The ﬁring of bricks are typically done in a tunnel kiln or less frequently in a periodic kiln, where the bricks are
ﬁred between 10−80 h, whereof the maximum temperature is kept for 2−6 hours (Andersen et al., 1989). The
ﬁring process can be divided into the following stages (Brick Industry Association, 2006b), I) the ﬁnal drying
from 200 −300 ◦C, II) dehydration from 150 −980 ◦C, III) oxidation from 540 −980 ◦C, and IV) vitriﬁcation
from 870 −1300 ◦C.
Different chemical, mineralogical and physical changes and processes occur in the material during ﬁring of
bricks, where some elements are being decomposed and new developed (Andersen et al., 1989; Brick Industry
Association, 2006b). During the ﬁring process, the material turns into a hard brick by sintering. The sintering
process is caused by to simultaneous processes: the melt down of minerals and development of new miner-
als(Andersen et al., 1989) .
The ﬁring rate is varying during the heating and cooling of the bricks, due to some speciﬁc processes during
ﬁring, which are vulnerable for the ﬁnal ﬁred product. These, together with a description of the mineralogical
development, are explained below. During the ﬁnal drying, the water bound in the pores evaporates, followed
by the mineralogical bound hygroscopic water which evaporates at ∼500 ◦C (Holmboe, 2001). The highest
amount of hygroscopic bound water is mainly found in high plasticity clay (clay with a high content of the
clay mineral smectite) (Krejbjerg et al., 1991). Oxidation of the organic material also occurs until ∼500 ◦C
(Romero et al., 2008). At 573 ◦C, the quartz inversion occurs which causes a volume expansion of ∼1% and
is reversible, why the heating rate often is lowered between 520 −620 ◦C Andersen et al. (1989). The break
down of the clay minerals begins at 500 −600 ◦C with kaolinite, followed by biotite, illite and smectite at
700 −900 ◦C. At 900 ◦C almost all clay minerals are decomposed, and the material begins to sinter Andersen
et al. (1989). At 700 −900 ◦C the carbonates, such as CaCO3, are being decomposed with the emission of
CO2 (Romero et al., 2008; Maitra et al., 2005). Andersen et al. (1989) states that the decomposition of carbon-
ates theoretically occurs at 894.4 ◦C, but in practice happens at lower temperatures, 550 −750 ◦C. At about
900 ◦C, quartz and feldspar begin to react and causes a liquid phase formation, without being decomposed
Andersen et al. (1989). According to (Segadaes et al., 2005) a typical clay behaviour is characterized by a
uniform expansion up to 900 ◦C, whereupon it is followed by an abrupt shrinkage. Of new developed minerals,
glass and haematite are the major compounds for ﬁred red clay bricks.
==PAGE:27==
5. Materials and Methods - Characterization
of marine sediments
For the production of ﬁred masonry units, ﬁne grained marine sediments (MS) from Sisimiut, Greenland, was
used to assess whether the occurrence is suitable for a brick production in Greenland.
The investigations of raw samples of MS from Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq, and the production and testing of
ﬁred specimen were divided into the following categories:
• Field work in Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq, Greenland
• Description of the raw materials used for production of ﬁred units
• Geotechnical investigations of MS
• Chemical investigations of MS
• Mineralogical investigations of MS and ﬁred samples
• Production of ﬁred brick pellets and testing of physical properties as a durability screening
• Determination of ﬁring behaviour and micro structure of ﬁred units
• Production of miniature bricks and testing of physical and mechanical properties
The sample collection and main part of the geotechnical investigation took place in Sisimiut (and Kanger-
lussuaq), Greenland, in August 2014. The mineralogy and chemical investigations were carried out in the
laboratory in building 119 at DTU, Kgs. Lyngby where the majority of the pellets also were produced and
ﬁred. The miniature bricks together with the third pellet production were formed and dried at DTU, but ﬁred
at the brickwork Wienerberger in Helsinge, Zealand.
The methods used for experimental work in this project are shown in Table 5.1. In this chapter the materials
used for the brick production and each experimental method / procedure is shortly described and accompanied
by relevant tables, ﬁgures and pictures. In Chapter 7 the thoughts behind the design of miniature bricks are
discussed.

5.1. FIELD WORK
17
5.1
Field work
The ﬁeld work took place in two Greenlandic towns, Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq, and was carried out from
August the 4-21st, 2014.
Fig. 5.1 shows how the samples of MS were collected and the equipment used. A snail drill was used by hand
for taking up samples of raw material in three different depths; 30 cm, 50 cm and 70 cm under surface level.
Organic material and surface soil with a high organic content was removed before drilling.
The samples were taken in different locations near both towns. The map in 5.2 shows the exact location of each
sample collected in Sisimiut. The map of sample locations in Kangerlussuaq together with the GPS coordinates
in degrees, minutes, and seconds (DMS) are shown in Fig. A.1 and Appendix A, respectively.
(a) Collecting samples of MS in Kangerlussuaq, Greenland
(b) Equipment: snail drill, marker, GPS and plastic bags for
storing the samples. Sisimiut
Figure 5.1: Samples of MS were collected near the towns Kangerlussuaq and Sisimiut, Greenland
Samples of MS from Sisimiut were used for a production of ﬁred units in the present study. The reason
for collecting samples of MS in Kangerlussuaq, was to be able to compare the geotechnical, chemical and
mineralogical properties with results from previous studies by Larsen et al. (2001); Sørensen and Welinder
(2003); Olsen and Johansen (2013); Alm and Brix (2005); Blok et al. (2007); Jacobsen et al. (2002) as a
control of the methods.
MS from Kangerlussuaq was considered less suitable and therefore not used for a production of bricks. This
was based on results and conclusions from previous studies. A high content of chlorides were found in MS
samples from Kangerlussuaq by Larsen et al. (2001); Sørensen and Welinder (2003); Olsen and Johansen
(2013); Alm and Brix (2005); Blok et al. (2007); Jacobsen et al. (2002), and ﬁred specimen made of MS from
Kangerlussuaq (and Sisimiut) were investigated with respect to the open porosity (OP) and water absorption
properties (WA) by Olsen and Johansen (2013). It was observed that ﬁred units of MS from Kangerlussuaq
had a higher open porosity and water absorption than ﬁred units of MS from Sisimiut. These two problems
were the reason for not focusing on the MS from Kangerlussuaq.
In the present study, the samples SIM Final (1-3) were used for a production of ﬁred specimen, and were
collected from a hole with the dimensions 70 cm in depth and 100x100 cm in widths excavated by hand by
using a shovel and a snail drill.
It was assessed that occurrences of MS located far away from the towns and infrastructure would not be relevant
to investigate due to the high economical expenses.

5.2
Materials
The raw material used in the production of ﬁred specimen involves ﬁne grained marine sediments (MS) from
Sisimiut, and a ﬁne grained residue from crushing of granitic rocks for aggregate production (CR), produced
at Betoncentralen, Nuuk, Greenland (Belmonte, 2014).
5.2.1
Fine grained marine sediments (MS)
The samples of MS from Sisimiut used for the production of ﬁred specimen (pellets and miniature bricks)
were taken from the location SIM Final, see location in Fig. 1.1. The location was chosen due to an occurrence
of MS close to the surface, and based on the results of a preliminary geotechnical investigation carried out
during the ﬁeld work period. Additional samples of MS were collected from other locations in both Sisimiut
and Kangerlussuaq to investigate the properties of as many raw samples as possible, and thereby analyse the
homogeneity of the occurrences. Results from the geotechnical, chemical, and mineralogical investigations are
shown for the samples SIM Final (1-3), SIM 2, SIM 6 and KAN 4, whereas results of the remaining samples
are shown in Appendix B. The samples SIM Final 1, 2 and 3 were collected from the same location but in
different depths. The different depths were investigated to ensure homogeneity with depth. Furthermore the
samples SIM 2 and SIM 6 were taken from different locations close to Sisimiut to ensure homogeneity of the
soil in the area. The sample KAN 4 was taken from Kangerlussuaq to compare with the results from previous
studies.
Dried and crushed sample of marine sediments from Sisimiut is shown in Fig. 5.3a.

5.2.2
Fine grained residue from crushing of granitic rocks (CR)
CR is a ﬁne grained residue from crushing of granitic rocks for aggregate production, which is produced at
Betoncentralen, Nuuk, Greenland (Belmonte, 2014). An addition of 10 % to 20 % of CR in the fraction
0.125−2.00 mm was used in the production of some of the ﬁred specimen. The material used as an addition in
bricks was investigated by Belmonte (2014), and it was found that an addition caused a lower loss on ignition
(LOI) and thereby a lower linear shrinkage of the ﬁred bricks.
The grain size distribution, the major element distribution (XRF), the bulk and clay mineralogy (XRD), and
loss on ignition (LOI) were as well determined by Belmonte (2014), and showed a d50 of 0.6mm. The XRF
analysis showed a high content of MgO, which was higher than the content of Fe2O3. The loss on ignition was
very low (1.7wt%). The bulk analysis by XRD showed a content of micas, amphibole, quartz, feldspar and
small peaks of talc.
5.3
Geotechnical investigation
A geotechnical investigation was made to classify the soil and to ensure the properties and homogeneity of the
soil with respect to the natural water content, the (apparent) bulk density, the particle density, the particle size
distribution, the plastic and liquid limit, and the plasticity index. The standard DS/CEN-ISO/TS-17892 (2004),
was used for the classiﬁcation.
Various samples from Sisimiut and one from Kangerlussuaq were investigated with respect to the geotechnical
properties. Results from the samples SIM Final (1-3), SIM 2, SIM 6 and KAN 4 are shown in the following
tables. For all results, see appendix Appendix B.
5.3.1
Natural water content
The natural water content was measured as the water content over the dry mass. The test was carried out
according to DS/CEN-ISO/TS-17892 (2004) Part 1. For the test, 25 g of wet sample was dried for 24 h at 105
◦C. The test was done in triplicate for all depths, and the water content calculated as w = mw
md · 100%, where
mw is the mass of the water in the specimen (g) and md is the mass of the dry specimen (g).
==PAGE:32==
20
CHAPTER 5. MATERIALS AND METHODS - CHARACTERIZATION OF MARINE SEDIMENTS
5.3.2
Bulk- and particle density
The bulk density of ﬁne-grained soil was determined by the method ”linear measurement”. The test was carried
out according to DS/CEN-ISO/TS-17892 (2004) Part 2. For the test, 50−80 g of material was dried for 24 h
at 105 ◦C and ground to a ﬁne powder. The material was added to a metal cylinder,where it was stamped 30
times to pack the grains and remove the air pores. The bulk density was determined by
ρ = (d/2)2πhsoil
(5.1)
where hsoil is the height of the soil in the cylinder, d the inner diameter of the metal cylinder.
To determine the particle density, a pycnometer test was performed. The grain density of the soil was used
for the determination of the settling velocity of the particles by Stokes equation, see Eq. (5.6). The test were
carried out in accordance with DS/CEN-ISO/TS-17892 (2004) Part 3.
For the test, 10 g of soil sample, dried at 105 ◦C for 24 h and ground into a ﬁne powder, was transferred to
the pycnometer and weighed, m2. A control liquid (distilled water) was added until 10-20 mm above the soil
sample. Enclosed air was again removed by subjecting the pycnometer to vacuum for 30 min, the pycnometer
with the saturated specimen was weighed, m3, and the particle density was determined as
ρs =
m4
(m1 −m0) −(m3 −m2) · ρw,
(5.2)
where m0 is the mass of a dry and clean pycnometer glass, m1 the mass of the clean pycnometer glass, and
ρw = 0.9978g/cm3 the density of the water at 20 ◦C.
Test were done in triplicate.
5.3.3
Grain size distribution
To determine the grain size distribution the following tests were carried out in accordance with DS/CEN-
ISO/TS-17892 (2004) Part 4
• Sieve analysis for the sand fraction (particles with d > 63 µm)
• Hydrometer test for the clay and silt fraction (particles with d < 63 µm)
The grain size distribution is of importance in the production of bricks since it has inﬂuence on the packing
arrangement of the grain when forming the bricks. An even grain size distribution is preferable according to
curve (Holmboe, 2001). For the test, approximately 120 g of moist soil was dried for 24 h at 50 ◦C. Peptiator
was added to the dry sample to separate the particles from each other, and left overnight. The dissolved soil
sample was sieved through a 63 µm sieve to separate the sand from the silt and clay fraction by adding pepti-
sator and gently using a bung. The sand fraction was dried and sieved through 2000 µm - 60 µm sieves.
The clay and silt fraction and peptisator was added to an Andressen pipette and gently shaked, see Fig. 5.4a.
The velocity of subsidence of the clay particles was determined by drawing off samples of 10 g with a decreas-
ing frequency from after 1 min to 24 h.
5.3.4
Plasticity of soil
To determine the plasticity of the soil a fall cone test and the Atterberg limits test were carried out. The tests
were made in accordance with DS/CEN-ISO/TS-17892 (2004) Part 6 and 12.
• The plastic limit, wP , is the water content at which a soil becomes too dry to be plastic and is empirically
determined from the Atterbergs limit test.
==PAGE:33==
5.4. CHEMICAL INVESTIGATIONS
21
(a) Hydrometer test
(b) Fall-cone test
(c) Atterbergs limit
Figure 5.4: Tests for determination of grain size distribution, liquid, and plastic limit
• The liquid limit, wL, is the water content at which the soil is too liquid to be plastic and is empirically
determined from the fall-cone test.
• The plasticity index (PI), IP , is the difference between the liquid limit and the plastic limit of at soil,
IP = wL −wP and determines the range of water contents where the soil exhibits plastic properties.
PI is of importance when forming bricks of the soil, since a low plasticity index entails a soil which is difﬁcult
to form and not very workable due to the small range of possible water contents where the soil behaves plastic.
For both the fall-cone test and the Atterberg limits test the soil was not sieved through a 0.4mm sieve since
the soil was considered sufﬁciently ﬁne-grained from the plots of the grain size distribution shown in Fig. 8.1.
Organic material and small stones were removed when found. A cone with the properties 60g/60◦was used for
the fall cone test (determination of liquid limit), see Fig. 5.4b. The penetration depth of the cone was measured
for the soil sample with different water contents.
For the determination of the Atterbergs limit (plastic limit), the soil was mixed with water until the consistency
of the soil was about ∼7 mm for the 60g/60◦cone, where the soil was supposed to be plastic. Small rolls of
soil with a thickness of 3 mm were rolled on a ceramic mixing plate, see Fig. 5.4c.
5.4
Chemical investigations
The determination of the chemical properties was made to make it possible to understand the behaviour of
the ﬁred brick product, and avoid unwanted elements in the raw material. In this section, the methods for
determination of the content of total carbon and sulphur (LECO), content of organic carbon (volumetric titration
with HCl), content of salts i pore water (IC), electrical conductivity, pH, and loss on ignition (LOI). All tests
were done in triplicate.
5.4.1
Total carbon and sulphur content
The contents of total carbon, TC, and sulphur, S, was determined by analysing the soil samples in a high fre-
quency oven, LECO CS-200. The content of organic carbon is of importance in the ﬁring process due to the
emission of heat and gasses, and since it may result in a more porous brick. A high content of sulphur can
cause efﬂorescence and discolourations on the surface of the ﬁred bricks (Madsen, 1983; Holmboe, 2001). For
the test, 0.5 g of sample was added to small ceramic crucibles and covered with accelerators to accelerate the
ignition of the sample, and to oxide the carbon and sulphur within 1 min.
Fig. 5.5a shows the ceramic crucible with sample and accelerators after ﬁring in a LECO CS-200.
==PAGE:34==
22
CHAPTER 5. MATERIALS AND METHODS - CHARACTERIZATION OF MARINE SEDIMENTS
(a) Sample after ﬁring in a LECO CS-200
(b) [Test set-up for determination of organic carbon (Schnell
and Ottosen, 2008)
Figure 5.5: Test set-ups for determination of total carbon, sulphur, and calcium carbonate
5.4.2
Content of calcium carbonate
The content of calcium carbonate CaCO3, was determined volumetrically by titration with HCl on a Shneibler
apparatus. All tests were done in triplicate. The test for determination of CaCO3 was carried out to make
a better understanding of the color of the ﬁred brick product, since it depends on the ratio between CaCO3
and Fe2O3 (Andersen et al., 1989), and to avoid ”lime blowing” caused by bigger grains of CaCO3 (Madsen,
1983; British Geological Survey, 2007).
To measure to content of CaCO3, the procedure by Schnell and Ottosen (2008) was followed and a Scheibler
apparatus was used, see Fig. 5.7. First standard curves were run on samples containing 0.05−0.30 g of CaCO3
before running the soil samples of 2.50 g. For the test, 20 mL of hydrochloric acids HCl10 % was added to a
reaction bottle, and the beaker with the present sample was added without letting it in contact with the HCl.
By shaking the reaction bottle to mix HCl and the sample for 1 min, the chemical reaction occurs
CaCO3 + 2HCl →CO2 ↑+Ca++ + 2Cl−+ H2O
(5.3)
The two glass tubes, C and D were regulated to identical levels of CO2, and the content of CaCO3 and the
organic, and inorganic parts, were calculated as
CaCO3 =
VCO2 · 100
msample

= (TC −TOC) · 8.33 = TIC · 8.33,
(5.4)
where VCO2 is the volume of CO2 which was read of the glass tubes C and D, and msample the mass of the soil
sample. It was assumed that the amount of inorganic carbon (TIC) was only found in CaCO3, why it could be
calculated based on the results of total carbon (TC). TOC is the organic part of the total carbon content (TC),
see Section 5.4.1.
5.4.3
Loss on ignition
The organic content in the soil was determined by loss on ignition (LOI), and was based on the weight loss
between 105 ◦C and 550 ◦C ﬁred for 1 h. The LOI, as the weight loss between 105 ◦C and 1000 ◦C for 2 h,
is, beside the oxidation of organic matter, related to the dehydroxylation of clay minerals, decomposition of
carbonates, sulphides, and hydroxides (Mezencevova et al., 2012).
==PAGE:35==
5.5. MINERALOGY
23
(a) Determination of organic content after heating to 1000 ◦C
(b) Samples on shaking platform
Figure 5.6: Loss on ignition
The procedure by Schnell and Ottosen (2008) was followed. Ceramic crucibles were used for the test, and
heated in a mufﬂe furnace for 1 h to remove all organic material. For the test, 2.5 g of dry, ground sample was
added to the crucible and ﬁred in the mufﬂe furnace for 1 −2 h. Samples were cooled down in a desiccator
before weighing and LOI was measured as
LOI = mdry −mignited
mdry
· 100,
(5.5)
5.4.4
Electrical conductivity, pH and content of salt in pore water
The conductivity of the soil was measured to give an indication of how well an electrical current can conduct
through a given liquid, which is connected to the content of ions and thereby salt in the soil. The results from
the conductivity test can therefore be compared with the content of salts in the soil.
The procedure by Schnell and Ottosen (2008) was followed. For the test, 10 g of dry sample, and 25 mL of
distilled water was added to a plastic vial and left on a shaking platform overnight. The pH and electrical
conductivity was measured on the samples 20 min after removing them from the shaking platform.
A high concentration of salt in the raw material used for brick production might cause surface efﬂorescence,
discolourations, and corrosion of the kiln during ﬁring (Madsen, 1983).
The samples used for determination of conductivity and pH, were also used for determination of the content of
salts in the pore water by ion chromatography analysis (IC). The vials with sample and pore water were left for
1 h to let the soil particles settle, and the pore water was separated from the soil particles through a ﬁlter. The
contents of chlorine, Cl−, and sulphates, SO2−
4 , were measured.
5.5
Mineralogy
An X-ray diffraction analysis (XRD) was carried out to identify the crystalline phases in samples of MS. The
method IRI (reference intencity ratio) was used for the semi-quantiﬁcation of the bulk mineralogy (Foged,
1979; Belmonte, 2014), whereas a qualitative identiﬁcation was made of the clay minerals. The concepts for
using XRD for identiﬁcation of minerals are described in Section 5.5.3.
==PAGE:36==
24
CHAPTER 5. MATERIALS AND METHODS - CHARACTERIZATION OF MARINE SEDIMENTS
The analyse was done on a PanAnalytical X’Pert PRO diffractometer, using Cu Kα radiation and accelerating
voltage and current of 45 kV and 40 mA.
(a) Test samples for bulk mineralogical analysis of ﬁred sam-
ples
(b) Test sample for analysis of clay minerals
Figure 5.7: Samples prepared for a mineralogical analysis by XRD
5.5.1
Bulk mineral analysis
The bulk mineral analysis was performed on samples of raw MS, and on samples of MS (and CR) ﬁred at
different temperatures. The samples were dried/ﬁred, and ground into a very ﬁne powder, before they was
added to a cylindrical metal plate as shown in Fig. 5.7a. Regarding the ﬁred pellets, the XRD analysis was
carried out to analyse the difference in break-down/development of minerals at different temperatures. The
samples of pellets with 100 % of MS ﬁred at 900 ◦C and 1050 ◦C, and pellet with 80 % of MS and 20 % of
CR ﬁred at 1025 ◦C were tested.
5.5.2
Clay mineral analysis
The clay mineral analysis was performed on particles < 2 µm from the sample SIM Final 2. The clay paste was
prepared by adding approximately 20 g of raw MS sample and 50 mL of distilled water to a plastic container,
placed on a shaking platform overnight to separate the grains from each other. Particles > 2 µm were separated
from the clay fraction by the separation method (Moore and Reynolds, 1997). The suspension was added to a
25 cm high pyrex glass and the settlement velocity of the particles was determined by Stoke’s law
VT = g(dp −d1)D2
18η
= 3.85m/s ⇔t = 200mm · VT = 14.4h
(5.6)
where d1 = 998.2kg/m3 is the density of water at 20 ◦C, dp = 2770kg/m3 the particle density determined
by the pycnometer test, D = 2 µm the diameter of the grains in the clay fraction and η = 1.003 kg/(ms) the
viscosity of the liquid. After 14.4 h, particles d > 2 µm had settled 20 cm why the topmost 20 cm of the
suspension was drawn off into a plastic container, whereupon it was ultra-centrifuged by 3000 revolutions/min
for 30 min, until the clay particles and the water was separated sufﬁciently to make it possible to pour the water
from the sample. For preparing the oriented clay mineral aggregate the two different methods were tried out,
the ”Glass slide method” and the ”Smear mount method” both according to Moore and Reynolds (1997). The
==PAGE:37==
5.5. MINERALOGY
25
suspension was in both methods applied to a glass microscope slide and were undergone the following four
treatments before analysed by XRD (Moore and Reynolds, 1997):
• A - air-dried condition, at room temperature
• G - ethylene glycol-solvated condition, at room temperature for 48 h
• 350 - heated up to 350 ◦C condition, in mufﬂe furnace for 2 h
• 550 - heated up to 550 ◦C condition, in mufﬂe furnace for 2 h
5.5.3
Identiﬁcation of minerals by X-ray diffraction
As stated, X-ray diffraction is used for identiﬁcation of crystalline minerals by their structure. X-rays are
deﬁned as electromagnetic radiation with a known wavelength of approximate 1.54 ˚A (for CuKα radiation),
which is typically smaller than the distance between the atomic planes in a crystal lattice e.g. a clay mineral or
other minerals as quartz, feldspar etc. (Moore and Reynolds, 1997; Holmboe, 2001).
(a) Reﬂection of x-rays from two
planes of atoms in a solid (Rutgers
and Atronomy, 2015)
(b) Schematic diagram of an X-ray diffractome-
ter (Rutgers and Atronomy, 2015)
Figure 5.8: Principles for X-ray diffraction
In Fig. 5.8a two incident waves (rays) are striking two atomic planes, and both waves are being reﬂected. For
the waves, the angle of incidence, θ, is equal to the angle of reﬂection, θ′. When incident waves strike a particle,
diffraction occurs in every possible orientation of 2θ. At certain incident angles constructive interference
will occur, which is when two or more rays are in phase and their amplitude is being incremented. When
this happens, and an enormous amount of mutually constructive, interfering waves are occurring, a diffracted
”beam” is produced (Moore and Reynolds, 1997). As observed in Fig. 3.1, the spacing between the atomic
planes for different clay minerals is well known, why they can be identiﬁed by X-ray diffraction. Based on
this, the distancebetween the two atomic planes, d, can be determined by Bragg’s equation, n · λ = 2dsin(θ),
where n = 1 is the order of the reﬂected wave, λ = 1.54 ˚A is the wavelength of the CuKα radiation , and θ
the angle of the reﬂected (scattered) X-ray (Moore and Reynolds, 1997). The outcome of an X-ray diffraction
is a so-called diffractogram, where the angle 2θ is pictured on the x-axis, and the intensity of different angles
is pictured on the y-axis. A peak symbolises a 2θ with a higher amount of reﬂected waves.
==PAGE:38==
26
CHAPTER 5. MATERIALS AND METHODS - CHARACTERIZATION OF MARINE SEDIMENTS
==PAGE:39==
6. Production and testing of ﬁred units
In this chapter, the procedures and considerations for how to produce small, ﬁred brick pellets and miniature
bricks of ﬁne grained marine sediments (MS) and crushed granite (CR) are described.
The purpose of a preliminary production and testing of small ﬁred brick pellets, was to use an easy method to
obtain knowledge about the durability properties, such as water absorption, of the ﬁred product. The results
made it possible to identify the most appropriate combinations of materials and production methods in a brick
production where MS was used as the main raw material. A screening of the pellets with different variables
in the composition of material and production method was carried out, and the variables concerned the initial
forming water content, content of CR, forming pressure, ﬁring temperature, and ﬁring procedure/time, and
were chosen in accordance with Chen et al. (2011). The parameters giving the seemingly best and most ap-
propriate results from the screening of pellet properties, were used for a production of miniature bricks. The
miniature bricks were produced and classiﬁed based on their resistance to freezing when wet by the require-
ments in the American standard, ASTM-C62 (2013).
Considerations about the production of miniature bricks such as the different options of forming methods, the
shape and design of a form produced speciﬁcally for the production of miniature bricks in the present study,
are demonstrated in Chapter 7.
The ﬁred products includes three productions of small ﬁred bricks pellets with a diameter and thickness of d∼
20 mm and h∼3, respectively and a dry mass of mdry ∼2 g ﬁred at DTU or at Wienerberger Tegl. The ﬁred
miniature bricks with the dimensions (b x l x h) 54 mm x 54 mm x ∼60 mm were ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl.
An overview of the different productions of brick pellets and miniature bricks is shown below and in Table 6.1.
Pellet production 1 with MS ﬁred at DTU
Variables: Initial forming water content, ﬁring temperature.
In Pellet production 1, pellets were made of 100 % MS from SIM Final, with two different initial forming water
contents, 10 % and 15 %. The pellets were formed by dry-pressing with a pressure of 47 MPa. The ﬁring was
conducted at DTU in a mufﬂon furnace, and pellets were ﬁred at four different maximum temperatures, 900◦C,
950◦C, 1000◦C and 1050◦C, with the maximum temperature kept for 1 h. The heating and cooling rates, and
the compression rate were not controlled.
Pellet production 2 with MS and CR ﬁred at DTU
Variables: Content of CR, ﬁring temperatures, compression pressure.
In Pellet production 2, pellets were made of MS from Sisimiut with an addition of three different contents
of CR, 10 %, 15 % and 20 %, and an initial forming water content of 10 %. The pellets were formed by
dry-pressing with two different pressure, 20 MPa or 47 MPa. The ﬁring was conducted at DTU in a mufﬂon
furnace, and pellets were ﬁred at two different maximum ﬁring temperatures, 1025◦C and 1050◦C, with the
maximum temperature kept for 1 h. The heating and cooling rates, and the compression rate were not con-
trolled.
Pellet production 3 with MS and CR ﬁred at DTU and Wienerberger Tegl
Variables: Firing procedure / ﬁring time, content of CR.
In Pellet production 3, pellets were made of MS from Sisimiut solely, with an addition of CR of 20 %, and an
initial forming water content of 10 %. The pellets were formed by dry-pressing with a pressure of 20 MPa. The
ﬁring of half of the pellets was conducted at DTU in a mufﬂon furnace, and pellets were ﬁred at a maximum
27
==PAGE:40==
28
CHAPTER 6. PRODUCTION AND TESTING OF FIRED UNITS
ﬁring temperatures of1025◦C, with the maximum temperature kept for 1 h. The heating and cooling rates,
and the compression rate were not controlled. The other half of the pellets were ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl in
Helsinge, where the ﬁring time was 3.5 days, and the maximum temperature ∼1020◦C.
Production of miniature bricks with MS and CR ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl
Production of ﬁred miniature bricks (54 mm x 54 mm x 60 mm) was made based on the results from the water
absorption tests of the pellets.
Type 100MS was made of 100 % MS, an initial forming water content of 10 %. Type 80MS20CR was made of
MS with an additional content of ﬁne-grained residue from crushing of granite (CR) of 20 %, an initial forming
water content of 10 %. Both types were formed with a pressure of 20 MPa, with a controlled compression rate.
The miniature bricks were ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl in Helsinge, where the maximum temperature was
∼1020◦C, and a controlled heating cooling rate similar to the rate for a common brick ﬁring at Wienerberger
Tegl (∼3.5 days). See Section 6.5.

6.1
Production of ﬁred brick pellets
In this section, the production of ﬁred brick pellets is described, which includes the preparation of raw material,
the forming method/pressure, and the drying and ﬁring process of the pellets. See Table 6.1 for the different
variables in the three pellet productions. The variables for the three pellet productions were chosen in accor-
dance with Chen et al. (2011), where the inﬂuences on the compressive strength and water absorption of the
ﬁring temperature (850−1050 ◦), forming pressure (10−30 MPa) and forming water content (5−20 %) were
investigated. The pellets were made in quintuple in the present study.
6.1.1
Preparation of raw material for pellets
For the preparation of the raw material, MS of the sample SIM Final was used for the pellet production. The
dimensions of the small brick pellets were chosen in accordance with Torres et al. (2009), with a dry mass of
==PAGE:41==
6.1. PRODUCTION OF FIRED BRICK PELLETS
29
(a) Fired miniature bricks and brick pellets. Left: 80 % MS
and 20 % CR. Right: 100 % MS
(b) Raw mixes of MS (and CR) with different water contents.
Figure 6.1: Outcome of brick production, and different compositions of raw material (MS, CR, water)
the pellet of approximately 2 g, and a diameter of approximately 20 mm. The procedure is listed below.
• MS was dried at 105◦C for 24 h, cooled down in a desiccator with silica gel, and ground into a ﬁne
powder in a mortar. Larger particles and organic material were removed.
• The powder of MS (and CR) was mixed with distilled water and left overnight in a sealed container to
absorb the water.
• The mix was divided into small plastic vials with material for one pellet each, mdry ∼2.0 g.
6.1.2
Forming of pellets before ﬁring
Forming the small brick pellets was a challenge due to a very low plasticity index of the raw material (IP =
4.3−9.3 %), see Table 8.3, which was also stated by Belmonte (2014); Olsen and Johansen (2013). Therefore,
it was decided to form the pellets by the dry-press method, (Torres et al., 2009; Brick Industry Association,
2006b). All the pellets were compressed by a hydraulic pressing machine, Stenhøj at DTU with a force of 14.8
kN (equivalent to a pressure of 47 MPa) or 6.2 kN (equivalent to a pressure of 20 MPa).
• In Pellet production 1, a forming pressure of 47 MPa was chosen to follow the same principles as in
Torres et al. (2009).
• In Pellet production 2 and 3, a forming pressure of 20 MPa was used for half of the pellets and chosen
in accordance with Gencel et al. (2013) to determine the inﬂuence of the compression pressure on the
durability.
A lower forming pressure for pressing the brick units was preferable, since a pressure of 47 MPa was assumed
unrealistically high and energy consuming for a real life brick production. The aspect of energy consump-
tion was considered by lowering the compression pressure. A visual investigation of pellets consisting 10 %
of water, 15 % of CR, and compressed with lower pressures of 10 MPa and 15 MPa was made. The lower
pressures showed that the material in the compressed pellet did not stick sufﬁciently together and appeared
too incoherent, which was another reason for choosing a compression pressure of 20 MPa. The pressing rate
was not controlled, but done manually in a hydraulic press, (Stenhøj, DTU). Fig. 6.2 shows the production
==PAGE:42==
30
CHAPTER 6. PRODUCTION AND TESTING OF FIRED UNITS
procedure of pellets.
The pressed, green pellets (before drying) were weighed and the diameter was measured in triplicate by a
micrometer scale gauge. After forming, pellets were dried at 105 ◦C for 24 h and again weighed and measured.
The drying shrinkage was determined as
