6.1.3
Firing of pellets
Pellet production 1, 2 and half of the pellets in production 3 were ﬁred in a mufﬂe furnace at DTU for 1 h at
the maximum temperature with a fast-ﬁring cycle (∼5 ◦C/min). The remaining pellets in Pellet production 3
were ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl following industrial procedures with a slow-ﬁring cycle (∼15 ◦C/h).
• In Pellet production 1, the pellets were ﬁred at four different temperatures of 900 ◦C, 950 ◦C, 1000 ◦C and
1050 ◦C to get an indication of the optimal ﬁring temperature. The maximum ﬁring temperatures were
chosen from previous investigation of moulded bricks made of Greenlandic MS from Illulisat (Belmonte,
2014), where the ﬁring temperature varied between 1030 ◦C and 1080 ◦C, and brick pellets of MS from
Sisimiut made by Olsen and Johansen (2013), ﬁred at 1000 ◦C. The lower temperatures were to chosen
in the hope for results showing sufﬁcient durability properties, since the lower ﬁring temperatures would
be less energy consuming.
• In Pellet production 2, the ﬁring temperature was optimized, so the pellets were ﬁred at two different
temperatures, 1025 ◦C and 1050 ◦C. The temperatures were chosen on the basis of the results from the
water absorption test of Pellet production 1, where 1050 ◦C showed the best water absorption properties.
==PAGE:43==
6.2. DURABILITY PROPERTIES OF FIRED PELLETS
31
The temperature of 1025◦C was chosen due to the maximum ﬁring temperature at Wienerberger Tegl
(1015 −1020 ◦C).
(The ﬁring temperature was set at 1025◦C, even though the ﬁring temperature at Wienerberger Tegl
is 1015 −1020 ◦C. The difference in ﬁring temperatures occurred due to a misunderstanding in the
communication with Wienerberger Tegl.)
• In Pellet production 3 the pellets were ﬁred for 3.5 days at 1015−1020 ◦C at Wienerberger Tegl following
the procedures used at the brickwork.
The mufﬂe furnace used for ﬁring the pellets was not able to reach temperatures above 1050◦C, nor it was
able to control the heating and cooling rates. Heating/cooling-curves were made by measuring the temperature
every 10 min until the maximum temperature was reached. In the cooling phase the temperature was mea-
sured every 10th min until approximately 800◦C was reached. Another reason for determining the heating and
cooling-curves, was to ensure the similarity with the ﬁring procedure of brick pellets by Olsen and Johansen
(2013). The ﬁring curves for the mufﬂe furnace at DTU are shown in ﬁgure Fig. 9.2, and the ﬁring curve for
Wienerberger Tegl, in Fig. 9.3.
After ﬁring, the pellets were left in a desiccator to cool down and then weighed, whereupon the ﬁring-, and
total shrinkage in weight and diameter could respectively be measured as

6.2
Durability properties of ﬁred pellets
In this section, the methods for determination of water absorption, open porosity and bulk density are described
by following the principles in two different standards, the American, ASTM-C67 (2014), and the Danish, Ti-B-
25 (1983). The resistance to freezing of the ﬁred brick pellets was indicated by the water absorption properties
(ASTM-C62, 2013). For a classiﬁcation of building bricks in different weathering grades, the requirements
in (ASTM-C62, 2013), apart from the water absorption, concern compressive strength, and calculation of the
saturation coefﬁcient.
The water absorption was determined as an indication of the best variables used in the pellet productions.
The variables in production methods, and composition of material, concern the ﬁring temperature, -time, and
-procedure, the initial forming water content, content of CR, and the forming pressure of the pellets, see
Table 6.1.
• The procedure in the standard ASTM-C67 (2014) was followed, since the weathering grade for the
miniature bricks were classiﬁed by this standard, which is explained in Section 6.6.
• The procedure in the standard Ti-B-25 (1983) from the Technological institute of Denmark was followed
to be able to compare with previous research of ﬁred brick pellets made of Greenlandic MS from Illulisat,
and Kangerlussuaq by Olsen and Johansen (2013); Belmonte (2014).
==PAGE:44==
32
CHAPTER 6. PRODUCTION AND TESTING OF FIRED UNITS
• The open porosity (OP) and bulk density (BD) were determined by following the procedure in Ti-B-25
(1983), and since no procedure for determination of OP and BD is stated in ASTM-C67 (2014), the same
equations as in Ti-B-25 (1983) were used. The difference between the water absorption results obtained
by the two methods, was analysed.
6.2.1
Water absorption, open porosity and bulk density of pellets
The water absorption of the ﬁred pellets were tested according to the procedure in ASTM-C62 (2013) / ASTM-
C67 (2014) and by following the principles in Ti-B-25 (1983). The procedures for the water absorption test
according to ASTM-C67 (2014) and Ti-B-25 (1983) are both described in Table 6.2.

6.3. SEM IMAGES OF DRIED AND FIRED UNITS
33
6.3
SEM images of dried and ﬁred units
Images were taken by a SEM (Scanning electron microscope) of the raw fracture surface of two types of pellets
after drying/ﬁring at different temperatures.
• Pellet of 100 % of MS, dried at 105◦C / ﬁred at 1050◦C.
• Pellet of 80 % of MS, 20 % of CR, dried at 105◦C / ﬁred at 1050◦C.
The images were taken to get an idea of the packing arrangement of the grains in a dried and a ﬁred pellet and
to analyse the difference between the two stages for pellets with different mixes of MS/CR. In Fig. 6.3a, the
dried/ﬁred sample is placed in the SEM before the analysis.
It was not possible to get an idea of the pore sizes / distribution by analysing the SEM images. For an analysis
of the pore sizes / distribution of the ﬁred unit, thin sections should have been made.
(a) Images by Scanning electron microscope (SEM)
(b) Vacuum-tight, horizontal pushrod dilatometer, DIL 402C
Figure 6.3: Scanning element microscope (SEM) and sintering test by DIL 402C
6.4
Sintering process for ﬁred units
The sintering process, measured as the thermal dilation of ﬁred specimen, was determined in a vacuum-tight,
horizontal pushrod dilatometer, DIL 402C. The dilation with respect to ﬁring time/temperature of pellets of
Greenlandic marine sediments was not measured in previous research. The dilatometer describes the thermal
expansion/shrinkage [%] and the rate of thermal expansion [% / mm] with respect to the heating and cooling
temperature [◦C].
• Sample type. The same compositions of raw material as used in the production of miniature bricks were
used, see Table 6.4. Brick type 100MS was made of 100 % MS and 10 % initial forming water, and brick
type 80MS20CR was made of 80 % MS, 20 % CR, and 10 % of initial forming water.
• Sample preparation. The samples used for the sintering test had a diameter of 5 mm and a length of
8 −10 mm and were prepared in a manual pressing machine. The compression pressure and the rate of
compression were estimated as unimportant for the sintering process.
• Firing curve. Sintering tests were carried out with three different ﬁring curves. The main part of pellets
were ﬁred in the mufﬂe furnace at DTU, where it was not possible to control the heating / cooling rates
==PAGE:46==

of ∼5 ◦C. Therefore three sintering tests were made to imitate the ﬁring curve from the mufﬂe furnace
at DTU (Test 1-3), to be able to determine the effect of the ﬁring procedure by lowering the heating
cooling rate around 573 ◦C, see explanation below.
The ﬁring procedure/curve at Wienerberger (the brickwork), is shown in Fig. 9.3, and is going on over
3.5 days with a much lower rate of heating and cooling.
• Lowered heating rate. One of the severe temperatures in the ﬁring process of red bricks is at 573 ◦C
where quartz changes its atomic structure from α-quartz to β-quartz. The change causes a reversible
volume expansion of approximately 1 % (Holmboe, 2001). For a more thorough explanation of the
ﬁring process, see Section 4.4. Therefore Test 4 and 5 were executed with a lower heating and cooling
rate of ∼2 ◦C in the range 520◦C - 620◦C in order to investigate the effect of a lowered heating cooling
rate in this temperature region.
The ﬁve different sintering tests with respect to the heating and cooling rates were conducted as described
below in Table 6.3. The heating- cooling curves are shown in Fig. 9.11, Fig. 9.12 together with the strain
development during ﬁring. The ﬁring curves in Test 1, 2 and 3 were simulating the ﬁring curve for the mufﬂe
furnace at DTU, whereas Test 4 and 5 had a lowered heating- cooling rate between 520◦C and 620◦C to avoid
cracks from the increase in volume for quartz.
6.5
Production of ﬁred miniature bricks
The raw materials for a miniature brick production, were prepared by the same principles as for the pellets. MS
was dried for 24 h at 105◦C and cooled down in a desiccator with silica gel. Then ground into a ﬁne powder
by centrifuging in a ”ring crusher” machine for 10 s.
A new form was developed to be able to form the miniature bricks by the same procedure as the small brick
pellets, the dry-press method was used in accordance with Brick Industry Association (2006b). Considerations
about the forming methods, shape, dimensions and design of a brick presser are discussed in Chapter 7
• The proportion of each material (MS, CR, water) was mixed and left in sealed plastic containers overnight
to absorb all water, see the different compositions of bricks in Table 6.4.
• The material was ﬁlled into the brick presser and compressed by a maximum load of 58.3kN (equivalent
to 20MPa) in accordance with the procedure in Gencel et al. (2013).
• The forming of the bricks was made on an Instron 5500R, 6025 compression machine at DTU, with a
compression rate of 2.00 mm/min from 0kN to 10 kN and of 1.00 mm/min from 10 kN to Fmax. The
extension - load curve is shown in Fig. 9.14.

Even though the best results from the pellet production was found for pellets formed by a pressure of 47 MPa,
the pressure of 20 MPa was chosen for the bricks. Due to the strain - load curve in Fig. 9.14 it seems possible
to lower the forming pressure down to approximately 20 −25 kN, since the curve begins to get much steeper
from this load.
The drying, ﬁring and total shrinkage in weight, widths and height was determined by the same methods and
equations as for the pellets, equation Eq. (6.1), Eq. (6.2), and Eq. (6.3), where x refers to the mass, the widths
or the height of the miniature brick.
6.6
Characterisation of ﬁred miniature bricks
The ﬁred miniature bricks were classiﬁed by the requirements in ASTM-C62 (2013) and tested by following the
procedures in ASTM-C67 (2014). The following tests were performed on the bricks to classify the weathering
grade, see Table 6.5:
• Water absorption: 24 h of submersion in water and 5 h of boiling according to ASTM-C67 (2014)
• Minimum compressive strength (ASTM-C67, 2014)
• Calculation of the saturation coefﬁcient (ASTM-C67, 2014)
Furthermore were the following properties determined:
• Open porosity following the principles in Ti-B-25 (1983) and ASTM-C20 (2010)/(ASTM-C67, 2014)
• Bulk density following the principles in Ti-B-25 (1983) and ASTM-C20 (2010)/(ASTM-C67, 2014)
The standard ASTM-C62 (2013) was followed, since it classiﬁes building bricks into different weathering
grades based on their resistance to damage by freezing when wet. The classiﬁcation in the standard is divided
into three grades of weathering, where the arctic climate goes under the severe weathering grade, Grade SW.
The physical and mechanical requirements for grade SW are listed in Table 6.5 and concern the Minimum
compressive strength, the Maximum water absorption and the Maximum saturation coefﬁcient.
Table 6.5: Values from table 1, Physical Requirements in ASTM-C62 (2013)

6.6.1
Water absorption and saturation coefﬁcient for miniature bricks
The ﬁrst part of the water absorption test, where units were submerged in cold water for 24 h, was determined
by the same method as described for the pellets, whereas the second part was followed to determine the water
absorption after boiling for 5 h.
• For determination of WA24c, the miniature bricks were dried, cooled, weighed (mdry), submerged into
cold water (15.5 −30 ◦C) for 24 h and weighed again under (msw.24c) and over water (msa.24c). WA24c
was calculated by Eq. (6.4).
• For determination of WA5b the miniature bricks used for the 24 h cold-water submersion test was,
without drying them in between, submerged into clean water (distilled) at a temperature between 15.5◦C
- 30◦C which was heated to boiling and kept for 5 h. After 5 h of boiling the water was cooled down to
a temperature between 15.5 −30 ◦C by natural loss of heat. The bricks were removed from the water
bath and weighed after wiping excess water from the surface (msa.5b) and under water (msw.5b).
• For determination of the open porosity, OP5b and bulk density, BD5b, the procedure for the water ab-
sorption test in the standard ASTM-C20 (2010) is different from the procedure in ASTM-C67 (2014).
According to ASTM-C20 (2010) the units shall be added to a water bath and boiled for 2 h, cooled
down to room temperature and kept in the bath for 12 h. The units should then be weighed under water
(msw.2b) and over water (msa.2b) after removing excess water from the surface. In spite of this differ-
ence, the results from the water absorption test after 5 h of boiling were used for determination of OP
and BD.

(b) Compression testing of miniature brick 80MS20CR

6.6.2
Minimum compressive strength for miniature bricks
The compressive strength was measured on a 60 ton hydraulic press. The miniature bricks were dried at 105
◦C for 24 h and cooled down before testing, in accordance with ASTM-C67 (2014). The compressive strength
was determined as the maximum applied stress (MPa) before breaking.

CHAPTER 6. PRODUCTION AND TESTING OF FIRED UNITS
==PAGE:51==
7. Development of form for miniature bricks
For the production of miniature bricks, a brick pressing form was designed. The considerations about the
methods of forming the miniature bricks, the shape, the dimensions, and the design of a pressing form are
discussed in this section.
7.1
Forming of miniature bricks
In previous studies of bricks made of MS from Greenland at DTU, hand-moulded or hand-shaped bricks were
produced by Belmonte (2014); Olsen and Johansen (2013); Sørensen and Welinder (2003), respectively. In this
research a method where green bricks could hold their shape before drying was wanted, and though different
options were considered, the dry-press process was chosen (Brick Industry Association, 2006b).
• Soft-mud process (Moulding). In connection with the research made by (Olsen and Johansen, 2013) a
small scale production of miniature bricks of MS from Sisimiut was made. The bricks were formed by
moulding in small, rectangular metal forms. Due to a very low plasticity index of the MS from Sisimiut,
Ip = 3.5 −5.8 % (Olsen and Johansen, 2013), it was requisite for the bricks to be dried in the metal
forms to hold their shape.
The method would not be very appropriate in a real life brick production because of a high consumption
of, time, material (metal forms), and manual work.
• Stiff-mud process (Mechanical extrusion). The possibility of shaping the miniature bricks by mechan-
ical extrusion at Wienerberger Tegl was considered, but skipped because of few possible dimensions of
the bricks. The extruder at Wienerberger was installed to shape real size bricks (L = 228mm, B = 108
/ 168mm, H = 54mm). For testing the durability of the bricks, a smaller size was wanted to make the
testing process easier. Furthermore a big amount of raw material (MS) would have been needed for this
method.
• Dry-press process. The method of shaping the miniature bricks by dry-pressing was considered as
the most appropriate option. The same method as used for pressing the pellets could be used and it
was possible to conduct the whole procedure at DTU. The disadvantages of the pressing method, is
that the method would not be realistic in a real life brick production due to the high demand of energy.
Furthermore no appropriate form was found at DTU, why a pressing form was designed.
If a future large scale production of bricks made of MS from Sisimiut (or from another Greenlandic loca-
tion), the material must extrude well enough to consistently form good unﬁred-brick units (Chou et al., 2001).
Therefore the stiff-mud process of forming the bricks by mechanical extrusion must be investigated in future
investigations if an implementation of a brickwork is proceeding.
The methods used at Wienerberger Tegl are the ”soft-mud process (Moulding)” and ”stiff-mud process (extru-
sion)”, where respectively 80 % and 20 % are used in the brick production at the brickwork.

7.2
Shape and dimensions of miniature bricks
According to ASTM-C62 (2013) the shape and size of the tested bricks shall be speciﬁed by the purchaser. In
the standard there are only requirements for the maximum values for the permissible variation in dimensions
(Table 2 in ASTM-C62 (2013)). Since there is no purchaser in this situation, the shape and dimensions were
determined based on other studies of bricks where durability to freezing was determined using the same stan-
dard, ASTM-C62 (2013).
A short review of articles using ASTM-C62 (2013) was made to determine a suitable shape and size of
the bricks graded by the requirement in ASTM-C62 (2013). The review concerned the research made by
Mezencevova et al. (2012); Chou et al. (2001); Demir (2006); Bogahawatta and Poole (1996); Karaman et al.
(2006a); Gencel et al. (2013), see Table 7.1.
A cylindrical shape was considered, but since there was not found any article where bricks were classiﬁed by
ASTM-C62 (2013), the shape was eliminated.
The dimensions were chosen in accordance with Mezencevova et al. (2012): 54 mm x 54 mm x (50 −100
mm). In the study by Mezencevova et al. (2012) the same tests were performed on the bricks as in this study
(Minimum compression strength, water absorption tests by submersion in water for 24 h and boiling for 5 h,
and calculation of Saturation coefﬁcient). The miniature bricks were shaped using a brick pressing form and
the height was set to approximately 60 mm depending on the mix of materials. The elements are shown in
Fig. 7.1.

CHAPTER 7. DEVELOPMENT OF FORM FOR MINIATURE BRICKS

8. Results - Characterization of marine
sediments
In the following sections, the results obtained from the investigations of the raw material explained in Chapter 5
and of the ﬁred units explained in Chapter 6, are presented using ﬁgures and tables. The raw data can be found
in Appendix B to Appendix G. In every subsection, the results from this research are discussed brieﬂy and
compared with requirements in relevant standards. In Chapter 10, the results are discussed more thoroughly
and compared with results from previous research.
8.1
Geotechnical investigation
The following sections present the results from the geotechnical investigations. The soil is characterized by
the plasticity index (PI) and the grain size distribution (GSD). Furthermore, the bulk and particle density of the
soil is shown.
Tables and ﬁgures with all results are shown in Appendix B.
8.1.1
Bulk density and particle density
The (apparent) bulk density was determined together with the particle density of samples of MS, both are
shown in Table 8.1. The bulk and the particle densities are both very similar for all samples, though the particle
density is slightly lower for KAN 4.

8.1.2
Grain size distribution
The grain size distributions (DSD) of the samples from Sisimiut are all dominated by the clay and silt fraction
(< 60 µm), and have a content of 28 % in the clay fraction, 55 % in the silt fraction, and 16 % in the sand
fraction, see Table 8.2. The shape of the curves in Fig. 8.1 show an even GSD, which is preferable according to
Holmboe (2001). A steep curve is and indication of a well sorted soil, where all grains have the approximately
same grain size. The curves in Fig. 8.1 are evenly distributed and relatively coinciding.
According to Figure 2.9 in Gribble and McLean (2003) the MS is determined as the soil type ”Silty clay loam”
which refers to a moderately ﬁne soil, which feels gritty, is sticky when moist and will ribbon (Plant and soil
sciences eLibrary, 2014).

8.1.3
Plasticity of soil
The results from the fall-cone test (liquid limit) and determination of Atterbergs limit (plastic limit) are shown
in Table 8.3. The plastic limit, wP , is at 12%, the liquid limit at 17 −20% for the samples from Sisimiut.
The plasticity index was found to be between 4% −9%. The samples from Kangerlussuaq were found to have
a higher plasticity index, which was expected due to the grain size distribution with a more dominating clay
fraction (Belmonte, 2014; Olsen and Johansen, 2013).
In Fig. 8.2b the A-line is deﬁned as IP = 0.73(wL −20) (Casagrande, 1947), and the plasticity index for the
samples is plotted against the liquid limit.

8.2
Chemical investigations
The samples of MS are characterized by the content of carbon (total, inorganic and organic), sulphur and
loss on ignition (LOI) in the raw MS samples, together with the contents of salts in pore water, the electrical
conductivity, and pH.
8.2.1
Content of carbon, CaCO3 and sulphur
The contents of total carbon (TC), sulphur, CaCO3, total organic carbon (TOC) and total inorganic carbon
(TIC) are shown in Table 8.4. The results in Table 8.4 show very low levels of all values. The standard
deviation (SD) for the content of TC, S, and CaCO3 is < 0.1 for all samples.

The contents of total carbon was found < 0.20wt% for the samples from Sisimiut and < 0.002wt% for the
sample from Kangerlussuaq. The content of total organic carbon was calculated as the carbon content stem-
ming from CaCO3, given that the amount of inorganic carbon only was found in this chemical compound.
==PAGE:58==
46
CHAPTER 8. RESULTS - CHARACTERIZATION OF MARINE SEDIMENTS
Another source of inorganic carbon could be other carbonates such as iron carbonate, FeCO3, which may give
account for a higher content of inorganic carbon. A low content of carbon is preferable in clay used in brick
productions due to development of gasses and a higher porosity due to the decomposition of organic carbon at
150 −315 ◦and of carbonates at 700 −900 ◦(Romero et al., 2008).
The content of calcium carbonate, CaCO3, is very low, which is consistent to the color of the ﬁred brick prod-
uct. A low content of CaCO3 gives rise to red bricks, which is consistent with the red colour of bricks of MS
from Sisimiut. The CaCO3 in the MS may come from fossils (Jacobsen et al., 2002).
The content of sulphur was found to be low for all samples, and slightly higher in the sample from Kangerlus-
suaq. For all samples the content of sulphur was < 0.5wt%. Higher levels of sulphur may cause efﬂorescence
on the masonry surface in the form of sulphates (Nielsen, 1993), as well as discoloration and formation of
gypsum.
8.2.2
Loss on ignition
The loss on ignition (LOI) of the samples is listed in Table 8.5, and shows a low LOI after heating to 550 ◦C,
which includes the contribution of organic matter, water bound in the pores, and mineralogical bound in the
minerals (Holmboe, 2001). The LOI after heating to 1000 ◦C is low as well, and includes furthermore the
contribution of carbonates, and the dehydrolyxation of the clay minerals. The standard deviation for LOI is
< 0.2 for all samples.
The low organic content is consistent with the inorganic origin of the marine sediments (Belmonte, 2014).
8.2.3
Electrical conductivity and pH
The electrical conductivity and pH of the pore water of MS samples are shown in Table 8.5.

The electrical conductivity is considerably higher for the sample from Kangerlussuaq, and is strongly related
to the salt content (amount of ions). The relatively high electrical conductivities reveal a high content of salt in
the samples. The standard deviation for the electrical is < 1.0 for all samples.
The pH-values are low of the samples from Sisimiut, and slightly higher for the sample from Kangerlussuaq.
All samples are on the acidic range, which is consistent with the low content of carbonates. The standard
deviation for pH is < 0.2 for all samples.

and 1000 −2500 mg/kg for sulphate. It is obvious, that the requirements for the chlorine concentrations are

the ﬁred units after submersion in water and drying.

was used in this project, which might cause differences in the XRD results. Minerals, such as micas (biotite,
illite), are softer than minerals as e.g. quartz, why it is presumable to ﬁnd a higher content of mica in the ﬁner
fraction.
The bulk mineralogy of ﬁred samples, and one dried sample, is displayed in Fig. 8.3 which shows various
changes between in the sample dried at 105 ◦C, ﬁred at 900 ◦C and 1050 ◦C. For the sample containing 20 %
CR it is obvious that a larger content of micas (biotite, illite), and presumably also quarts and feldspar, was
present.
• Quarts was present in all samples, and is revealed by the strong peak at d = 3.23 ˚A, no matter the ﬁring
temperature, though the intensity was lowered with increasing ﬁring temperature. The sample of 80 %
MS and 20 % CR had a higher content of quarts.
• Feldspar was present in all samples, and is revealed by the strong peak at d = 3.20 ˚A, no matter the
ﬁring temperature. The same tendency as with quarts was observed, and the content of feldspar decreased
more than quarts at the higher temperatures.
• The content of micas (biotite, illite) is largest for the sample of 80 % MS and 20 % CR, and was
represented by the peak at d = 10.0 ˚A. The peak was as well identiﬁed for the unﬁred sample of 100 %
MS (SIM Final 2), whereas it was almost not present for the sample of 100 % MS ﬁred at 900 ◦C, and
completely dehydroxylized in the pellet of 100 % MS ﬁred at 1050 ◦C.
• Amphibole is represented by the peak at d = 8.4 ˚A in the unﬁred sample of 100 % MS (SIM Final 2),
and in the sample of 100 % MS ﬁred at 900 ◦C. For samples heated to 1025 −1050 ◦C no amphibole
was present.
The peak at d = 2.59 ˚A in the sample of 100 % MS ﬁred at 900 ◦C, as well represents amphibole and
disappeared at 1050 ◦C.
• The change in peaks representing feldspar was probably due to a phase change from low-temperature
feldspar to high-temperature feldspar.

• The d = 14.5 ˚A peak on the air dried sample which changed position to d = 16.0 ˚A identiﬁed a content
of smectite, an expandable clay mineral (Moore and Reynolds, 1997).
• Chlorite was identiﬁed from the presence and increase of the d = 14.0 ˚A peak and the disappearance of
the second order d = 7.0 ˚A reﬂection on the 550 ◦C heated sample.
• illite and biotite were identiﬁed from the d = 10.0 ˚A peak on all four samples, though the intensity was
lower for the air-dried and ethylene-glycol solvated samples. The increase in intensity was considered
due to a decrease in the distance between layers, caused by a collapse when heated. The d = 10.0 ˚A
peak should be identical, however a displacement of the 550 ◦C heated sample was observed.
• Amphibole was identiﬁed from the d = 8.5 ˚A peak. The peaks in all four samples should be identical,
however a displacement of the 550 ◦C heated sample was observed at d = 10.0 ˚A. The content of
amphibole, together with quarts and feldspar (not shown in the plot), conﬁrmed the presence of non-clay
minerals in the clay fraction < 2µm.
• The disagreement in the location of the peaks (d = 10.0 ˚A and d = 8.5 ˚A) was considered due to
instrument variations.

9. Results - Fired pellets and miniature bricks
In the following sections, results related to the production and investigation of ﬁred units are shown.
9.1
Production of ﬁred brick pellets
This section comments on the three different productions of brick pellets that have been made. In the following
sections, the shrinkage in mass and diameter of the pellets is described as well as the durability properties as
water absorption, open porosity and bulk density. See Table 6.1 for an overview of the three productions of
pellets.
(a) Matrix of the ﬁrst production of pellets with varying ﬁring temperature (900◦C, 950◦C,
1000◦C and 1050◦C) and water content (10% and 15%)
(b) Pellets from the second production ﬁred at 1050◦C were melted into the ceramic ﬁring plates
Figure 9.1: Outcome of Pellet production 1 and a part of production 2
9.1.1
Mixing of raw materials and pressing of pellets
The relevant comments on the mixing and pressing of brick pellets are listed below. Fig. 6.1b shows the mix
of different compositions of raw material for the pellet production.
• In Pellet production 1 (varying water contents and ﬁring temperatures), the raw mix for the pellet with
respectively 10 % and 15 % of water, appeared very different. The mix with 10 % of initial water
51
==PAGE:64==
52
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS - FIRED PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
appeared as a loose powder while the mix with 15 % of water appeared as cohesive clods.
The difference in the two states was conﬁrmed by the results for the plastic limit, which was found to be
approximately 12 %. For MS mixes with a water content below 12 % the material would behave powder
like whereas it would behave plastic and would be mouldable for a water content > 12 % (Gribble and
McLean, 2003).
• In Pellet production 2 (varying contents of crushed granite (CR), compression pressures and ﬁring tem-
perature) the mix with 10 % of water appeared almost like the mix from the ﬁrst production of pellets,
but due to the low ability of absorbing water of CR, the mix appeared more moist. Different compres-
sion pressures were tried out in order to ﬁnd the lower limit of compression pressure of the pellets. A
compression pressure of 10 MPa was found to be too porous and incoherent. On the basis of this visual
investigation of the compression pressure, the pressure of 20 MPa was kept for the types of pellet with a
lower compression pressure.
• In Pellet production 3, the procedures were as described for Pellet production 1 and 2.
For all productions the appearance of the pellets was greyish. All types of pellets released some excess water
during pressing.
An additional comment is, that the pressing procedure for the ﬁrst production of pellets was different from the
second and third. For the ﬁrst production of pellets a circular piece of water-repellent paper was placed on
top of the bottom metal plate in the pellet presser. To make the paper stick to the surface of the plate, a bit of
distilled water was added between the bottom plate and paper. Some of the water may have entered the pellet
mix during pressing. In Pellet production 2 and 3 the water-repellent paper was considered unnecessary since
the pellets were found easy to remove from the form after pressing.
9.1.2
Firing of pellets
The pellets were ﬁred at different maximum temperatures and the outcomes of the different pellet productions
are described below.
• After ﬁring the pellets from Pellet production 1 at different temperatures, the colour of the pellets varied
from pale brown (900◦C) to a much stronger reddish brown (1050◦C), see Fig. 9.1a.
• After ﬁring the pellets from Pellet production 2 at different temperatures 1025◦C and 1050◦C, the pellets
ﬁred at 1025◦C appeared reddish brown with small glittering pieces of crushed granite (CR). The pellets
ﬁred at 1050◦C had melted down in the ceramic plates they were ﬁred on. The surface appeared glass
like and with a dark brown color. The majority of the pellets were not possible to get of the ceramic
plates. See Fig. 9.1b.
• In Pellet production 3 there was no signiﬁcant difference in the appearance between the pellets ﬁred in
the mufﬂe furnace at DTU and the pellets ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl. The ﬁring curve for the pellets
and miniature bricks ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl is shown in Fig. 9.3 and the whole ﬁring procedure lasts
approximately 3.5 days. The x-axis shows the car position which changes every 2.5 h, whereas the
maximum temperature (∼1020◦C) is held for 5 h.
The ﬁring curves are shown in Fig. 9.2 and were made by registering the temperature every 10min. The results
are shown for Pellet production 1, and curves are made for the ﬁring with a maximum ﬁring temperature of
900◦C, 950◦C, 1000◦C and 1050◦C. For Pellet production 2 and 3, the consistency of the ﬁring curves were
controlled each 30min. The heating and cooling rates were almost identical for the different temperatures and
furthermore almost identical with the ﬁring used for ﬁring brick pellets by Olsen and Johansen (2013), where
brick pellets were ﬁred at 1000◦C. The values were considered comparable due to the similar ﬁring curves.

9.2
Properties of brick pellets
In the following sections, the properties related to the durability of the pellets are shown, which are the shrink-
age in mass and diameter, water absorption, open porosity, and bulk density. The results of Pellet production 1
and 2 are plotted in ﬁgures, whereas results of Pellet production 3 are shown in Table 9.1.
9.2.1
Shrinkage of pellets
Drying-, ﬁring-, and total shrinkage of pellets were measured, though only the results of the total shrinkage are
illustrated in the following sections. Tables and graphs for drying-, and ﬁring shrinkage are shown in Appendix
E. The total shrinkage, is the shrinkage in mass and diameter between the pressed and ﬁred unit.
In Pellet production 1, the loose edges of the pellets weren’t ”cleaned/removed” after pressing. This gives a
certain level of uncertainties for the shrinkage in diameter, since especially the dry pellets were difﬁcult to
measure due to the rough edges.
In Pellet production 2, the pellets ﬁred at 1050 ◦C had created melt during the temperature increase from 1025
◦C to 1050 ◦C. The pellets had therefore melted down in the ceramic plates and it was not possible to determine
the mass. The diameter was measured in the extent it was possible.
Total shrinkage in diameter
The total linear shrinkage (TS) (in diameter) is shown in Fig. 9.4 for Pellet production 1 and 2. For Pellet
production 3, the results are shown in Table 9.1

• For Pellet production 1 (varying temperature and initial forming water content), the TS in diameter
increased with the ﬁring temperature, but was only considerable for ﬁring temperatures of 1050◦C (1.2%)
where the densiﬁcation was initiating. For temperatures between 900 −1000 ◦C the ﬁring shrinkage in
diameter was assessed as almost constant (0.1 −0.7 %) (see Appendix E).
The TS in diameter was slightly higher for a water content of 15 %, though the two curves had almost
identical shapes for the different initial water contents (10 % and 15 % of water). This indicates that the
free water even pressed out of the pellets during pressing or had evaporated during the drying.
• In Pellet production 2 (varying content of CR, forming pressure and ﬁring temperature), the TS in diam-
eter was slightly decreasing with increasing content of CR, and were for pellets ﬁred at 1025◦C for both
compression pressures, close to 0 %. This was expected, since CR was added to lower the shrinkage of
the ﬁred product. For a CR content of 10 −15 % the TS in diameter was just positive (0.1 %), whereas
it was slightly negative for a CR content of 20 % (−0.1 to −0.2 %).
The pellets ﬁred at 1050◦C had shrunken 4 −5 % in diameter and the change in shape was clear (The
total shinkrage is not shown in Fig. 9.4.) Fig. 9.1b illustrates the pellets ﬁred at 1050◦).
• For Pellet production 3 (varying content of CR and ﬁring procedures), the TS in diameter was higher for
the pellets of 100 % MS than for pellets with 80 % MS, 20 % CR, and were speciﬁcally ∼0.4 % and

Total shrinkage in mass
The total shrinkage (TS) in mass is shown in Fig. 9.5. Mainly because of the uncontrolled compression rate in
the production of pellets the shrinkage in mass was measured with large uncertainties.
• For Pellet production 1, the TS in mass was obviously largest for the pellets with 15 % of initial forming
water content. The gap between the two almost parallel curves was 0.2 −0.7 %, except for pellets with
a ﬁring temperature of 950 ◦C with a water content of 15 %, the total shrinkage increased with ﬁring
temperature.
• For Pellet production 2, the TS in mass was increasing with increasing content of CR, except for pellets
made with a compression pressure of 47 MPa and a CR content of 20 %. The shrinkage in mass increased
from 7.8 −9.3 %.
• For Pellet production 3, the TS in mass corresponded more or less with the results from Pellet production
2. The total water content was found to 8.0 −9.6 %. The shrinkage was slightly higher for pellets ﬁred
at WB, which was expected to be due to the longer ﬁring time.
The ﬁring shrinkage in mass was found to be consistent with the LOI of 2.1 % after ﬁring to 1000 ◦C for 2 h.
The sample used for determination of LOI was a dried, loose MS powder, where all elements were easier to
ignite, and was therefore expected to be higher than the ﬁring shrinkage in mass for the compressed pellets.
9.3
Durability of ﬁred brick pellets
In this section, the results for water absorption, open porosity, and bulk density are described. The properties
were determined based on procedures by two different standards, ASTM-C67 (2014) and Ti-B-25 (1983). The
aim of using two standards was to analyse the difference in the results, since ASTM-C67 (2014) does not
contain equations for the open porosity and bulk density. Previous investigations of durability of ﬁred brick
pellets were done by Belmonte (2014); Olsen and Johansen (2013) where the principles in Ti-B-25 (1983) were
followed.
It was expected that Ti-B-25 (1983) would give the largest values of water absorption and open porosity and
the lowest for the bulk density, since the pellets were kept in a vacuum-tight desiccator while the water was
added, which was expected to enhance the water entering the smaller pores. The pellets were kept in the water
bath for 24 h in the procedure by ASTM-C67 (2014), where they were only required to be submerged in the
==PAGE:68==
56
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS - FIRED PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
water bath overnight by Ti-B-25 (1983), see Table 6.2 for differences in the two methods.
For Pellet production 1 and 2, both methods for determination of the water absorption were carried out, but
since there was a good correlation in the results by the two different standards, the method by Ti-B-25 (1983)
was assessed to be unnecessary for Pellet production 3.
The minimum and maximum values for each pellet production (1-3) of the total shrinkage in diameter and
mass, open porosity, bulk density, and water absorption are given in Table 9.2.

• For Pellet production 1, the WA decreased with the ﬁring temperature. For pellets ﬁred at 900 ◦C there
was no correlation in the results, though it was difﬁcult to make any conclusion based on such few data
points. The WA for the ﬁring temperatures 950 ◦C and 1000 ◦C were almost identical (∼12%), but
decreased for a ﬁring temperature 1050 ◦C (∼10.5%). The lower WA for pellets ﬁred at 1050 ◦C was
expected due to the larger shrinkage.
There was no signiﬁcant difference between the results by method ASTM-C67 (2014) and Ti-B-25
(1983).
==PAGE:69==
9.3. DURABILITY OF FIRED BRICK PELLETS
57
• For Pellet production 2, there was a clear difference in the WA for the two compression pressures. A
compression pressure of 47 MPa gave a WA of 11.5 −10.3 %, where for a forming pressure of 20 MPa
it was between 13.1 % and 11.8 %. Furthermore, the water absorption decreased with increasing content
of CR for both forming pressures, but for the forming pressure of 47 MPa the impact of CR content was
more distinct.
The pellets with a content of CR ﬁred at 1050 ◦C were burned into the ceramic ﬁring plates, and were
therefore not included in the water absorption test.
The results by the method in ASTM-C67 (2014) gave a slightly higher water absorption than the method
in Ti-B-25 (1983). This was unexpected since the pellets were exposed to vacuum in Ti-B-25 (1983),
which would induce the water to penetrate pores smaller pores than without vacuum.
• For Pellet production 3, the water absorption test was executed by the procedure in ASTM-C67 (2014).
There was no signiﬁcant difference in the WA of pellets ﬁred in the mufﬂe furnace at DTU and at
Wienerberger Tegl.
The pellets containing CR were found to have a lower WA, than the pellets only containing MS (∼12.2
MPa / ∼14.2 MPa, respectively). The same tendency was observed when comparing the pellets from
Pellet production 1 and 2, compressed by 47 MPa, and ﬁred at 1000 −1050 ◦with and without an
addition of CR.
Due to the lower compression pressure of 20 MPa, the pellets with 100 % of MS in Pellet production 3,
had a higher degree of water absorption than in Pellet production 1, where the compression pressure was
47 MPa (∼14.2 MPa and 11.2 −12.4 MPa, respectively for pellets ﬁred at < 1025 ◦). As observed in
Pellet production 2, the compression pressure had a great inﬂuence on the water absorption properties.
The requirement for the maximum water absorption for the weathering grade ”Severe Weathering” (SW) in
ASTM-C62 (2013), is 17 % for 24 h submersion in cold water and 5 h of boiling. The water absorption
fulﬁlled for the requirement for grade SW with respect to the water absorption, but since the pellets only were
submerged in cold water for 24 h and not boiled, the values were not directly comparable, but could give an
indication of the water absorption properties for the miniature bricks.
The two methods, ASTM-C67 (2014) and Ti-B-25 (1983), gave sufﬁciently similar results to be able to use the
values found by ASTM-C67 (2014) to determine the open porosity by the principles in Ti-B-25 (1983).
9.3.2
Open porosity of ﬁred pellets
The graphs for open porosity (OP) in all three productions of pellets followed the shapes of the graph for water
absorption, but with a higher standard deviation due to the uncertainties when weighing the pellets under water.
The graphs for the open porosity are shown in Fig. 9.7.
The durability of the pellets increases with decreasing open porosity.
• For Pellet production 1, the OP decreased with increasing ﬁring temperature, though it was almost con-
stant for ﬁring temperatures 900 −1000◦C. For a ﬁring temperatures 900 to 1000◦C the open porosity
was ∼25% and for 1050◦C, ∼22.0%.
• For Pellet production 2, the OP for pellets pressed with a compression pressure of 20 MPa was 27.0 −
25.2% and for 47 MPa 24.5 −22.2%, decreasing for increasing content of CR. For a similar forming
pressure as in Pellet production 1 of 47 MPa, the OP was lower for the pellets with CR. Pellet production
1 and 2 were not totally comparable due to the different ﬁring temperatures.
• For Pellet production 3, the OP was lowest for pellets containing CR. OP was almost similar for the two
different ﬁring procedures.

9.3.3
Bulk density of ﬁred pellets
As for the open porosity the equation for the bulk density (BD) contains the mass of the saturated sample under
water, which induces uncertainties. The graphs for the bulk density are shown in Fig. 9.8.

• For Pellet production 1, the BD of pellets containing 10% forming water increased approximately lin-

==PAGE:71==
9.3. DURABILITY OF FIRED BRICK PELLETS
59
pressure of 47 MPa the increase was highest for between a CR content of 10 % and 15 %, whereas a
very small decrease took place between 15 % and 20 % of CR.
The BD for pellets in Pellet production 1 and 2 were found to be almost identical.
• In Pellet production 3, BD was clearly higher for pellets containing CR, than for pellets only containing
MS. This observation was not made when comparing the results from Pellet production 1, 2.
9.3.4
Partial conclusions about the properties of pellets
The following partial conclusions with respect to total shrinkage in diameter and mass, water absorption, open
porosity and bulk density can be made:
• The ﬁring temperature seems to have a great inﬂuence on the properties. Increasing ﬁring temperature
leads to higher shrinkage in diameter, lower water absorption and open porosity.
• Addition of CR induces lower ﬁring temperatures due to a higher degree of sintering.
• Increasing content of CR lowers the shrinkage in diameter, and a tendency of lower water absorption and
open porosity were observed.
• The forming pressure has a large inﬂuence on the properties. Higher forming pressure leads to more
durable pellets.
• No great difference observed in pellets ﬁred at DTU and at WB, where industrial procedures were fol-
lowed.
• Great uncertainties in determination of shrinkage in mass (water content), probably due to the pressing
procedure
9.3.5
SEM analysis
SEM images were taken of the raw fracture surface of dried pellets, pellets ﬁred at DTU and at Wienerberger.
The images with a zoom of 800x are shown in Fig. 9.9 and Fig. 9.10.
Images with a zoom of 37x, 800x, and 2500x were taken of pellets in dry condition and pellets ﬁred at different
temperatures at DTU and Wienerberger. See all images displayed in Appendix F. The images displayed in
Fig. 9.9 and Fig. 9.10 are
• Pellet dried at 105 ◦C, and pellet ﬁred at 1050 ◦C at DTU, 100 % MS, zoom x800
• Pellet dried at 105 ◦C, and pellet ﬁred at 1050 ◦C at DTU, 80 % MS, 20 % CR, zoom x800
The images of pellets of 100 % MS in Fig. 9.9 showed a homogeneously packed fracture surface of the dried
as well as of the ﬁred pellet. It is peculiar that there was almost no difference to observe between the dried and
the ﬁred pellet. The grains in the dried pellet appeared to have a more pulverised surface than the grain in the
ﬁred pellet. The more edged particles in the ﬁred pellet might be due to an initial melting of the grains.
From the shrinkage in diameter and water absorption test it was obvious that a change happened in the material
when it was ﬁred at 1050◦C, though this was not possible to observe.
On the contrary there was a great difference to observe in the images of pellets of 80 % MS and 20 % CR in
the dry and ﬁred condition, Fig. 9.10. The pellets ﬁred at 1050 ◦have a micro structure with very few open
pores and signiﬁcant signs of vitriﬁcation. The vitriﬁcation was furthermore observed by the appearance of the
glassy surface.

9.3.6
Sintering analysis
A thermo-dilatometric analysis of the ﬁring/sintering behaviour on a DIL 402C was made on ﬁve different
combinations. See Section 6.4 and Table 6.3 for an overview of the ﬁve tests. Test 1-3 were ﬁred by imitating
the ﬁring curve for the mufﬂe furnace at DTU and Test 4-5 were ﬁred with a lowered heating and cooling rate
520 ◦C and 620 ◦C. The sample in Test 1, 2 and 4 was a mix of 100 % of MS and 10 % of initial forming water
(Fig. 9.11), whereas the sample in Test 3 and 5 was a mix of 80 % of MS, 20 % of CR and 10 % of initial
forming water (Fig. 9.12).
The development of linear dilation of the pellets is described below with a focus on ”shoulders” on the dilation
curves, such as at 573 ◦C where quartz causes a reversible change in volume of ∼1 % (Holmboe, 2001), at the
maximum dilation at 900 ◦C and during the 1 h of ﬁring at Tmax.
The important values for the ﬁve tests are shown i Table 9.3.
In Fig. 9.11 and Fig. 9.12 the ﬁring time is displayed as the x-axis, the dilation in length is read off the left
y-axis and the dilation curves displayed as unbroken lines, whereas the ﬁring temperature is read off the right
y-axis and ﬁring curves displayed as broken lines.
• Test 1. During the heating, a linear increase in dilation took place from 0% to 6.5 · 10−3 % until the
quartz inversion at 573 ◦C where an abrupt increase in dilation from 6.5·10−3 % to 8·10−3 % occurred.
An increase at a lower rate found place until ∼900 ◦C where the maximal dilation was found (9.4·10−3
%). A fast decrease in the dilation during the 1h of ﬁring at the maximum temperature (1050 ◦C) until
the quartz inversion in the cooling phase from -1.5 · 10−3% to −2.5 · 10−3 %, which was smaller change
in volume than for the heating phase. The ﬁnal dilation at 200◦C was found to be −5 · 10−3 %.
• Test 2 (compared to Test 1). The dilation curve for Test 2 approximately followed the curve for Test
1, but with higher dilations (1.0 −1.8 · 10−3 % higher), even though they should have been identical

• Test 4 (compared to Test 1 and 2). The lowered heating and cooling rate around the quartz inversion at
573 ◦C caused a smaller expansion for Test 4 than for Test 1 and 2. The maximum dilation at ∼900 ◦C
was a bit smaller for Test 4, but the dilation during the ﬁring at maximum temperature for 1 h and in the
cooling phase was almost identical with Test 2. Around 573 ◦C, the shrinkage was smaller, but due to
the prolonged ﬁring time, the ﬁnal shrinkage was a bit higher for Test 4 than for Test 2.
• Test 5 (compared to Test 3). In the heating phase until 573 ◦C, the development in dilation was almost
identical with Test 4 (identical ﬁring curves). The expansion at approximately 573 ◦C was smaller than
for Test 3. The maximum dilation was slightly smaller than for Test 3, but no big difference was observed
between the two tests.
It was observed, that every time there was a change in the heating or cooling rate, it affected the dilation a
little bit. This is revealed as small ”shoulders” on the dilation curves. Moreover, the lowered heating cooling
rate caused a lower development in the dilation in the temperature range 520 −620 ◦C, which was expected.
The samples containing CR ended up with an expanded volume whereas the samples with 100 % of MS were
shrunk. This was also observed for the two pellet productions (production 1 and 2). The lowered heating
cooling rate did not have much inﬂuence on the dilation of the ﬁnal product, which was also observed in Pellet
production 3, for pellets ﬁred at DTU with a fast ﬁring-cycle, and at Wienerberger Tegl with a slow ﬁring-cycle.
Finally, the ﬁnal shrinkage of samples ﬁred at 1050 ◦C were much higher than for the samples ﬁred at 1025
◦C. This was as well observed for the ﬁred brick pellets (Pellet production 1).
9.4
Production of ﬁred miniature bricks
In this section, aspects of the production procedure as pressing the bricks and the ﬁring procedure are discussed.
In the following sections, the results for the linear shrinkage and shrinkage in mass of the miniature bricks are
illustrated, as well as the durability properties as water absorption (24 h cold water and 5 h of boiling), open
porosity, bulk density, and compressive strength.
The miniature bricks were made in quintuple with two different mixes of material
• Type 100MS with 100% MS, and 10% of initial forming water.
• Type 80MS20CR with 80% MS, 20% CR and 10% of initial forming water.
Both types of miniature bricks were pressed with a compression pressure of 20MPa, and pressed by the same
pressing procedure, ﬁred at a maximum temperature of 1015−1020◦C by the same procedure at Wienerberger
Tegl. The only variation was the content of CR.
==PAGE:76==
64
CHAPTER 9. RESULTS - FIRED PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
9.4.1
Pressing of miniature bricks
The Instron 6025 was used for pressing of the miniature bricks, where the compression rate was set to 2
mm/min from 0 −10 kN and 1 mm/min from 10 −58 kN. The Load-Strain curves for each type of miniature
bricks are shown in Fig. 9.14, and show that the miniature brick 80MS20CR (containing 80% of MS, 20% of
CR) deformed more than miniature brick 100MS (containing 100% of MS). The height of brick 100MS was
hwet ∼61.5mm, whereas it was hwet ∼57.5mm for brick 80MS20CR. The difference in compressed height
may be due to the higher density of CR and packing arrangement of the grains. Moreover, the miniature brick
80MS20CR (containing 20% of CR) appeared more moist and more excess water was released, which was also
observed for the pellets.

9.4.2
Firing of miniature bricks
The miniature bricks were ﬁred at the brickwork, Wienerberger, following industrial procedures. Unfortunately
it was only possible to ﬁre the bricks at a maximum temperature of 1015 −1020◦C, and not at 1025◦C as
expected. The ﬁring curve is shown in Fig. 9.3, and each ﬁring lasts for 3.5 days.
9.4.3
Shrinkage of miniature bricks
The linear shrinkage of the miniature bricks was measured in three dimensions. An average of the three
shrinkages was calculated as the shrinkage in volume, since the variance between the lengths was found to be
minor. The total shrinkage in mass is equal to the LOI. The shrinkages are displayed in Table 9.4 where the
values for the identical (same mix and production procedure) brick pellets are shown.
The total linear shrinkage was highest for Brick 100MS, which was expected since the CR was added to reduce
the shrinkage due to the observations by Belmonte (2014). However, the linear shrinkage was negligible for
both brick types. This is considered to be due to the high compression pressure of 20 MPa and the low forming
water content when forming the bricks. Furthermore, a very low LOI was found in the samples, which induces
a lower shrinkage. According to Brick Industry Association (2006b), the drying shrinkage is usually falling
within the range of 2 −4 %, and the ﬁring shrinkage within the range of 2.5 −4 %. The linear shrinkage of
the miniature bricks was found to be much lower than these levels. Using a lower compression pressure when
forming the bricks, was considered and is recommended for future studies, since a higher linear shrinkage
would be acceptable.

==PAGE:79==
10. Discussion - Properties of marine
sediments
In the following sections, the results are discussed more thoroughly and compared with results from previous
research of ﬁne grained marine sediments from Greenland. See Table 10.1. Additionally, investigations of
bricks from different countries with respect to durability properties and ﬁring behaviour are compared to the
results. The inﬂuence of variables in the production are discussed.
10.1
Geotechnical investigation
The geotechnical properties are discussed and compared with results from previous investigations of Green-
landic marine sediments, shown in Table 1.2. The sample ”SIM Final”, was taken from the same location
as the sample ”Main” in the study by Olsen and Johansen (2013) and the results should therefore be almost
identical. Belmonte (2014); Foged (1979) investigated a great amount of samples of marine sediments from
locations on the Greenlandic west coast, and concluded that particle size distribution, mineralogy, and major
element distribution were very similar in all samples. Furthermore, Belmonte (2014) concluded that the same
things counted for marine sediments from Canada and Norway.
The plastic properties for marine sediments from different locations around the Greenlandic west coast were,
according to Belmonte (2014), found to vary much more, and to be within 3 −45 % for the plastic limit,
and 18 −70 % for the liquid limit. This places MS from Sisimiut in the lowest range of both the liquid and
plastic limit, which was furthermore found to have the lowest plasticity index. This was as well concluded by
the plasticity results of different marine sediments from Greenland from previous investigations by (Olsen and
Johansen, 2013; Belmonte, 2014; Sørensen and Welinder, 2003; Jacobsen et al., 2002; Alm and Brix, 2005),
shown in Table 10.1. Foged (1979) reported higher plasticity indices for marine sediments found in other areas
of Sisimiut, the Pamap Kua area and Parassuaq basin (PI = 8.6−18.3 %). According to Ovesen et al. (2012),
a plasticity index (PI) from 4 −7% is found for a silty, strongly clayey soil or a clayey, strongly silty/sandy
soil. For PI > 10% the soil is considered as clayey. In Fig. 8.2b, the plasticity chart plotted in accordance with
Casagrande (1947) shows that all samples from Sisimiut are placed in the CL- (inorganic low plasticity clay)
and ML-region (inorganic low plasticity silt). This corresponds to the results from the grain size distribution
where the soil was categorized as ”Silty clay loam” (Gribble and McLean, 2003). The sample from Kanger-
lussuaq was positioned in the ML-region (inorganic low plasticity silt).
The low workability of the samples were consistent with the presumably low content of smectite found by the
qualitative analyse of the XRD results (The content of smectite is unknown, but presumed to be low. Smectite
is a clay mineral that has the ability to contain a high degree of bound water, which makes the soil plastic and
formable (Holmboe, 2001)).
In the forming of the bricks, the dry-pressed method (Brick Industry Association, 2006b) was chosen, based
on the low PI, where low plasticity clays are mixed with up to 10 percent of water.
As for the grain size distribution, MS from Sisimiut was dominated by the silt fraction, and were found to
be coarser than MS from Kangerlussuaq, and Illulisat, and more or less in agreement with Ameragdla. This
corresponds to the plastic properties, where materials with a high clay fraction have propensity to behave more
plastic, due to a possible higher content of clay minerals (especially smectite) (Holmboe, 2001). According to
Mezencevova et al. (2012) silt is a non-plastic material and is considered as a problematic material with respect
67

10.2. CHEMICAL INVESTIGATIONS
69
to forming, due to the low plasticity. The grain size distribution ﬁts nicely with results by Olsen and Johansen
(2013).
10.2
Chemical investigations
The content of total carbon (TC) and sulphur (S) corresponds well with previous studies of investigations of
marine sediments from Sisimiut and Kangerlussuaq (Olsen and Johansen, 2013; Belmonte, 2014; Sørensen
and Welinder, 2003; Jacobsen et al., 2002; Larsen et al., 2001; Alm and Brix, 2005) and will not lead to any
problem related to production of bricks. The low content of total carbon, is associated with a low content of
organic carbon which is preferable in brick clay. As indicated by the result of total carbon, the content of
calcium carbonate, CaCO3 is as well low, and will therefore not cause any problems related to ”lime blowing”
(Madsen, 1983). If the raw materials contain particles of CaCO3 > 0.25 mm, they would, when ﬁred, trans-
form to CaO (quicklime) with liberation of CO2, which may cause lime blowing and cracks. Quicklime may
cause an expansion and thereby cracks in the ﬁred bricks (Andersen et al., 1989).
The low content also corresponds with the reddish colour of the ﬁred units, since a ratio between CaCO3 :
Fe2O3 < 3 gives red bricks (Andersen et al., 1989). Jacobsen et al. (2002) found a higher content of CaCO3,
and the colour of bricks of MS from Kangerlussuaq appeared more pale-red.
In this study, the organic content was measured as the loss on ignition (LOI) after ﬁring for 1 h to 550 ◦C,
and MS from Sisimiut was found to have the lowest organic content (< 1.7 %) of the MS shown in Table 10.1.
The same was the case for LOI after ﬁring for 2 h to 1000 ◦C (< 2.6 %), where the highest value was found in
MS from Illulisat (5.1 %). LOI for all the Greenlandic MS samples were within the low range for red-clay ma-
terials and are associated with decomposition of organic matter and volatile components (Acchar et al., 2006).
Other clays/sediments with a higher LOI were used as brick clay by Romero et al. (2008); Acchar et al. (2006);
Dondi et al. (2002); Pontikes et al. (2007), and the higher LOI was not considered problematic. According
to Mezencevova et al. (2012) a certain amount of organic content is wanted in the raw material, since it can
contribute to a larger plasticity.
The chloride content in MS from Sisimiut was found to be 1600 −3000 mg/kg, which is considered as high
for materials used for brick clay (Madsen, 1983), and was not expected, since Olsen and Johansen (2013)
found very low contents in MS from Sisimiut. However it was observed, that the results agreed well with other
studies of MS samples from Kangerlussuaq and Ameragdla (Sørensen and Welinder, 2003; Jacobsen et al.,
2002). A suggestion for further studies could be to investigate the chloride concentration of samples of MS
from different locations in Greenland by the same method.
A high concentration of salt (e.g. chlorides) can result in surface efﬂorescence and cracks in the ﬁnished prod-
uct. Clay with more than 0.1 % chloride content is usually not suitable for bricks (Madsen, 1983). However,
no salt efﬂorescence was observed on the dry brick units after water absorption tests. Furthermore, the chlo-
ride may evaporate during ﬁring, which is problematic for the kiln due to corrosion (Madsen, 1983; Andersen
et al., 1989). According to Mezencevova et al. (2012); Romero et al. (2008) the negative effects of salts can be
neglected by adding an adequate efﬂorescence restraining additive such as BaCO3.
Olsen and Johansen (2013) stated that the high chlorine content was not considered an issue in the brick pro-
duction according to two experts at two independent brick production plants.
Sulphates can cause discolourations on the surface, and the low contents in MS from Sisimiut, is assessed to
be unproblematic due to the low concentrations (1.7 −18 mg/kg ∼0.00017 −0.0018 wt%). Brick clay with
a sulphate content > 0.5 wt% is, according to Madsen (1983); Andersen et al. (1989) considered unsuitable.
The result, furthermore, corresponds nicely with the results by Olsen and Johansen (2013).
==PAGE:82==
70
CHAPTER 10. DISCUSSION - PROPERTIES OF MARINE SEDIMENTS
10.3
Mineralogy
The qualitative bulk mineralogy corresponded well with the results by Olsen and Johansen (2013); Belmonte
(2014); Sørensen and Welinder (2003); Jacobsen et al. (2002); Alm and Brix (2005). The content of feldspar
(K-, Na-, and Ca-feldspar) was slightly higher for MS from Sisimiut, than the content in other Greenlandic
MS, and content of quarts slightly lower. See Table 10.1. In spite of the differences, the results are very similar
and the differences are considered to be due to uncertainties in the semi-quantitative analysis.
The qualitatively determined clay minerals in sample ”SIM Final” were illite, smectite and chlorite, which is
similar to results by Belmonte (2014); Alm and Brix (2005); Morelli and Bennedsen (2005); Jacobsen et al.
(2002) (except the content of kaolinite, which, however, was very low).
10.4
Suitability of MS from Sisimiut as brick clay
The assessment of whether marine sediments from Sisimiut are suitable as raw material in the production of
building bricks, based on the geotechnical, chemical and mineralogical properties, summarizes to:
• The low plasticity index induces limitations in the forming of bricks (Brick Industry Association, 2006b).
• The grain size distribution is well-sorted, which is preferable, due to the packing arrangement of the
grains (Holmboe, 2001).
• The low content of carbon and sulphur together with CaCO3 is preferable in brick clay due to emissions
and lime bloating (Madsen, 1983).
• The low content of CaCO3 corresponds to the reddish color of the ﬁred units (Andersen et al., 1989).
• The low LOI is preferable due to lower ﬁring shrinkage of the bricks. Though organic content contribute
to a greater plasticity (Mezencevova et al., 2012).
• The high concentration of chlorine is unwanted due to efﬂorescence on the surface of the ﬁred product
and to corrosion of the kiln when ﬁred (Madsen, 1983).
• The low content of sulphates is preferable, since it may cause efﬂoresce on the ﬁred brick (Madsen,
1983).
• The quantitative bulk-, and qualitative clay mineralogical composition corresponds to other types of
marine sediments from Greenland which have been assumed suitable as brick clay.
==PAGE:83==
11. Discussion - Fired brick pellets and
miniature bricks
In the following sections, the results from the testing of ﬁred brick pellets and miniature bricks are discussed.
To compare durability properties found in this study with previous studies of ﬁred units made of Greenlandic
marine sediments, an overview is given in Table 11.1 and Table 11.2.
Since not all the physical properties fulﬁlled the requirements of ASTM-C62 (2013), the effect of different
variables in the production of brick pellets are discussed and compared to results in other scientiﬁc papers.
This is done in order to improve the quality of the bricks, so they, with some improvements/changes in the
production method and material composition, can fulﬁl the requirements for the severe weathering grade by
ASTM-C62 (2013).
In this study, the variables in the production of ﬁred units are the ﬁring temperature, -procedure and -time,
the forming water content (the water mixed with the raw material before forming), forming pressure (when
dry-pressed), and the addition of CR.
11.1
Durability properties of ﬁred brick pellets and miniature bricks
The minimum and maximum values of the durability of pellets are shown in Table 9.2 for each pellet produc-
tion (1-3). All the measured properties are found to be within the range of other studies of ﬁred clay-based
brick pellets, formed by the dry-press method (Torres et al., 2009; Romero et al., 2008; Belmonte, 2014).
Regarding the miniature bricks, the shrinkage, water absorption open porosity, and bulk density are comparable
with the properties of ﬁred brick pellets produced with the same composition of material, ﬁring temperature,
forming pressure, and a signiﬁcantly shorter ﬁring time. The properties are shown in Table 9.2. This is an
important observation, since the properties of brick pellets can be used as a strong indication of the properties
of miniature bricks, as long as the same production method is used. The production procedure of brick pellets
is much easier to conduct, than the production procedure of miniature bricks. It is not necessary to ﬁre the
pellets at a brick work, and can easily be done in the mufﬂe furnace at DTU, without lowering their quality
signiﬁcantly.
The drying-, ﬁring-, and total linear shrinkage of all the ﬁred units were found to be very low (−0.2 to 1.3 %,
and (5.1) % the pellets that had produced melt i Pellet production 2) for brick pellets. Only the pellets ﬁred at
1050 % were found to have a total linear shrinkage within the range according to Brick Industry Association
(2006b), where it is stated that the drying and ﬁring shrinkage usually are within the range of 2 −4 %, and
2.5 −4 %, respectively. The low forming water content, the dry-pressing forming method, and the low organic
content were considered to be the reason for the low shrinkage.
The bulk density of 1920 −2170 kg/m3 is within the range of ﬁred ceramic clay units by Torres et al. (2009),
though in the high end compared with ceramic clay units investigated by Dondi et al. (2002); Acchar et al.
(2006); Chen et al. (2011); Romero et al. (2008); Belmonte (2014). The high forming pressure, and thereby
the dense packing og the grains, are considered to be the reason for the high bulk density.
As seen in Table 9.6, the requirements for building bricks exposed to severe weathering (SW), stated in ASTM-
C62 (2013), were fulﬁlled for the minimum compressive strength, and the water absorption after 24 h submer-
sion in cold water and 5 h of boiling. The requirements for the saturation coefﬁcient (where the water absorp-
tion after 24 h submersion in cold water is included), were not fulﬁlled for the severe weathering grade (SW),
71
==PAGE:84==
72
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION - FIRED BRICK PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
nor the moderate weathering grade (MW). Due to this observation it will be necessary to improve to water
absorption properties of the bricks, and to approach the most appropriate production method and composition
of the bricks.
11.2
Fired units made of Greenlandic marine sediments
Fired units of Greenlandic marine sediments, were in previous projects investigated by Belmonte (2014); Olsen
and Johansen (2013); Sørensen and Welinder (2003); Narud (2006); Jacobsen et al. (2002). Very different
forming methods, forming water contents, and ﬁring temperatures and -times were used for the production,
why the results are difﬁcult to compare.
Belmonte (2014); Olsen and Johansen (2013) produced the same type of small brick pellets (d = 20 mm,
h = 3 mm) following the same procedures with respect to ﬁring temperature (1000 ◦C), forming pressure (47
MPa), and forming water content (10 %) of MS from Illulisat, Kangerlussuaq and Sisimiut.

The results are shown in Table 11.1 and shows that ﬁred pellets made of MS from Sisimiut have higher bulk
density, lower open porosity and water absorption compared to pellets of MS from Kangerlussuaq and Illulisat.
The shrinkage was largest for the Illulisat pellets. Even though the pellets of MS appear to be the most durable,
the problems related to the very low plasticity index is a challenge when forming the bricks.
Fired units of larger dimensions were made by Belmonte (2014); Sørensen and Welinder (2003); Jacobsen et al.
(2002) of MS from different locations in Greenland, and the production methods and durability properties are
shown in Table 11.2. The bricks of MS from Kangerlussuaq (Jacobsen et al., 2002) were ﬁred at the same
ﬁring temperature as the miniature bricks in this research, though formed by mechanical extrusion. According
to F´odi (2011) this forming method gives a lower compressive strength than if dry-pressed. Compared with
this type of brick, MS from Sisimiut are assessed to have a larger potential as brick clay due to the lower water
absorption and higher compressive strength. In Table 11.1, it is also seen that pellets of MS from Kangerlussuaq
have a higher water absorption than pellets of MS from Sisimiut. The bricks of MS from Ameragdla (Sørensen
and Welinder, 2003) had a water absorption very similar to the WA of the miniature brick type 100MS in
this project. Since the ﬁring temperature is unknown, it is difﬁcult to make any conclusion regarding the
durability of the bricks of MS from Ameragdla. The bricks of MS from Illulisat (Belmonte, 2014) were ﬁred
at higher temperatures, had a higher forming water content (the precise forming water content is unknown,
but was within the range of the plastic and liquid limit, ∼20 −20 %), and were hand moulded. The higher
ﬁring temperature, water-, and organic content resulted in a higher linear shrinkage of the bricks. The higher
ﬁring temperature was assessed to be one of the reasons for the superior durability properties. Based on this
knowledge, the most apparent improvement in the production of bricks of MS from Sisimiut, would be to
increase the ﬁring temperature. While the ﬁring temperature is increased, the linear ﬁring shrinkage must be

the ﬁring shrinkage can vary from clay to clay, but is commonly within the range 2.5 −4.0 %.
