11.3
Durability of ﬁred units - inﬂuence of variables
In this project it was investigated how different variables in the production of ﬁred units affected the durabil-
ity. The inﬂuence of the following variables were investigated and is in the following sections discussed and
compared with results from other researches concerning the durability of ﬁred units.
• Firing temperature, procedure and ﬁring time
• Forming water content and forming pressure (when dry-pressed). Furthermore alternative forming pro-
cedures were considered.
• Additional material, such as a ﬁne grained residue from the crushing of granitic rocks (CR))
The results of durability testing of ﬁred brick pellets and miniature bricks showed that
• Increasing ﬁring temperature and forming pressure has a large inﬂuence on the durability properties. It
led to a decrease in water absorption, open porosity and increase in linear shrinkage and bulk density.
• There was no signiﬁcant difference between properties of pellets ﬁred by a slow-ﬁring cycle at Wiener-
berger Tegl, and fast-ﬁring cycle in the mufﬂe furnace at DTU.
• Increasing forming water content did not have much inﬂuence on the water absorption properties, though
it resulted in a higher linear shrinkage.
• An addition of ﬁne grained residue from he crushing of granitic rocks (CR) resulted in a lower water ab-
sorption, open porosity, and higher bulk density. Furthermore the addition reduced the linear shrinkage.
Compressive strength was slightly lower for the ﬁred units containing CR.
In the following sections the inﬂuence of each variable is discussed and compared with results from scientiﬁc
papers. It must be noted that the different variables in the production of ﬁred units were investigated on the
small brick pellets, why their inﬂuence on the compressive strength is not included. The compressive strength
was only determined for the miniature bricks, where the only variable was the content of CR.
==PAGE:86==
74
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION - FIRED BRICK PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
11.3.1
Durability - Inﬂuence of ﬁring temperature
The inﬂuence of the ﬁring temperature was investigated in Pellet production 1, where brick pellets were ﬁred at
maximum temperatures from 900 −1050 ◦C. The open porosity and water absorption properties were almost
constant in the temperature range 900 −1000 ◦C, whereas they improved signiﬁcantly when ﬁred at 1050 ◦C.
See Fig. 9.7, and Fig. 9.6.
In Pellet production 2, some of the pellets containing CR were ﬁred at 1050 ◦C. However, the pellets started
to produce melt during the ﬁring and were impossible to get off the ﬁring plates. According to Holmboe
(2001) the amount of created melt and development of new mineral phases increases with increasing ﬁring
temperature, and the porosity and water absorption decreases and the compressive strength also increases. If
the amount of melt in the ﬁred unit exceeds 30 −50 %, the brick will begin to loose its form (Holmboe, 2001).
The inﬂuence of ﬁring temperature was also investigated by Chen et al. (2011) in a production of ﬁred units
made of clay from Wuhan, China. The durability was tested i.e. the water absorption and compressive strength.
The same effects of the ﬁring temperature was observed, as the WA decreased from 19.5 −15.2 % with
increasing ﬁring temperature (850 −1050 ◦C). Furthermore, Chen et al. (2011) investigated the inﬂuence
of the forming pressure (10 −30 MPa), and forming water content (5 −20 %), and found that the ﬁring
temperature caused the largest decrease in water absorption and an increase in compressive strength (15.5−26
MPa), followed by the forming pressure.
Karaman et al. (2006a,b) also concluded that increasing ﬁring temperature (700 −1100 ◦C) caused a decrease
in water absorption (19.5 −10.5 %) and increase in compressive strength (9 −31 MPa) of brick of clay from
Tokat-Turha Cayli, Turkey.
11.3.2
Durability - Inﬂuence of ﬁring procedure and time
Two different ﬁring procedures of the pellets were investigated. One fast-ﬁring cycle (∼5 ◦C/min) at DTU,
and one slow-ﬁring cycle (∼15 ◦C/h) at Wienerberger Tegl. No consistent difference was observed in the
open porosity, water absorption or bulk density for the pellets in Pellet production 3. The linear shrinkage in
diameter was slightly higher for pellets ﬁred at Wienerberger, but the only conclusion that could be made was,
that the longer ﬁring time made an insigniﬁcant difference for the properties of the pellets, shown by the almost
identical results from the water absorption testing.
The ﬁring behaviour of the pellets is shown by the thermo-dilatometric curves in Fig. 9.11, Fig. 9.12 and
Fig. 9.13. The effect of lowering the heating/cooling-rate (from 5−2 ◦C/min) around the quartz inversion (573
◦C) was assessed to be very small. A more thorough explanation is given in Section 11.4.1.
Miniature bricks were only ﬁred at Wienerberger Tegl with a slow-ﬁring cycle (∼15 ◦C/h). The difference in
properties for bricks ﬁred at different ﬁring times (120 −480 min) was investigated by Karaman et al. (2006a)
and no positive development for the water absorption nor the compressive strength was observed.
This was also conﬁrmed by (Saleiro and Holanda, 2012), where no variance was found in the linear shrinkage,
water absorption, apparent porosity, and apparent density of bricks ﬁred at a slow-ﬁring cycle (1 ◦C/min) and
fast-ﬁring cycle (20 ◦C/min). SEM images revealed that the slow-ﬁred pieces had a micro structure similar to
that of fast-ﬁring ceramic pieces (Saleiro and Holanda, 2012).
It was therefore concluded that a shorter ﬁring time was appropriate for preliminary testing of the small brick
pellets, as their immediate properties corresponded with the properties of pellets ﬁred at a slow-ﬁring cycle.
With respect to the ﬁring of bricks of larger dimensions, a longer and slower ﬁring time is necessary, since a
too fast ﬁring may cause a development of cracks, e.g. due to the quartz inversion at ∼573 ◦C (Holmboe,
2001).
According to Andersen et al. (1989) the common ﬁring time at Danish brickworks is 2 −3 days, and the
maximal ﬁring temperature kept for 2 −6 h. Within this period the physical and chemical processes are not
completed and the ﬁnal, ﬁred product will appear weaker, if ﬁred in a shorter period. A too fast ﬁring could
furthermore results in unwanted cracks due to surface shrinkage.
==PAGE:87==
11.3. DURABILITY OF FIRED UNITS - INFLUENCE OF VARIABLES
75
11.3.3
Durability - Inﬂuence of forming pressure and method
In the forming of the brick pellets and miniature bricks, the dry-pressed method was used in accordance with
Brick Industry Association (2006b). This method was primarily chosen due to the very low plasticity index of
MS from Sisimiut (IP = 4 −8 %).
The effect of the forming pressure was investigated in the pellet productions, where pellets were formed by
a pressure of 47 MPa and 20 MPa. It was clear, that the forming pressure had a large inﬂuence on the water
absorption (and the open porosity and bulk density, but only the water absorption is discussed here). For
pellets of 100% MS, the water absorption decreased from 14.4 % (pressure of 20 MPa) to 11.9 % (pressure
of 47 MPa). The difference was less distinct for pellets containing CR (Pellet production 2), where the water
absorption was 11.8 −13.2 % (pressure of 20 MPa, CR content 20 −10 %), and 10.2 −11.7 % (pressure of
47 MPa, CR content 20 −10 %).
The miniature bricks were formed by a pressure of 20 MPa in accordance with Gencel et al. (2013). The
forming pressure curve of the miniature bricks is illustrated in Fig. 9.14 as the deformation plotted against
the compression pressure. The effect of the compression pressure decreased intensely after a compression of
∼20 −25 kN (equivalent to ∼7 −9 MPa). It would therefore be interesting to investigate different (lower)
forming pressures of the miniature bricks, to optimize it regarding a lower consumption of energy.
The tendencies for construction bricks with an addition of haematite tailings, found by Chen et al. (2011), were
that the compressive strength increased with increasing forming pressure, though only up to a pressure of 20
MPa, whereupon the effect was negligible. F´odi (2011) furthermore states that the forming method has a high
inﬂuence on the compressive strength. It was concluded that extruded bricks obtained a compressive strength
twice as high as the moulded bricks, 77.9 MPa and 36.5 MPa, respectively.
According to Brick Industry Association (2006b), the recommended forming water content is 10−15 % for the
stiff-mud process (extrusion), which makes the bricks suitable for this method. In future studies, the extrusion
method should be carried out on the bricks to investigate if this method is more appropriate than the dry-
pressing method. If mechanical extrusion is used, it will presumably result in weaker and less durable bricks
if other variables as ﬁring temperature and composition of mixture are maintained. The extrusion method also
needs more water mixed into the raw material before forming (Brick Industry Association, 2006b). Vieira et al.
(2004); Dondi et al. (2002) state that the optimal plastic properties for forming bricks by mechanical extrusion
is within IP = 20 −25 %, and wP = 15 −25 %.
A large linear total shrinkage (13.8 %) of hand moulded bricks of MS from Illulisat was observed by Belmonte
(2014). The shrinkage was considered to be due to the forming method and thereby the higher forming water
content (unknown, but between 20 −30 %). An important observation is, that the water absorption is much
lower for these bricks, than for the miniature bricks of MS from Sisimiut, even though the dry-pressed forming
method was used. The bricks of MS from Illulisat were ﬁred at larger temperatures (1045 −1055 ◦C). This
shows, that the ﬁring temperature has a greater effect on the water absorption properties, than the forming
method, which was supported by the conclusion by Chen et al. (2011).
11.3.4
Durability - Inﬂuence of forming water content
The forming water content (the water mixed with the raw material before forming) is an important factor
for the durability and quality of bricks, since a too high forming water content may result in surface cracks
(Mezencevova et al., 2012), deformed bricks, and higher porosity (Chen et al., 2011).
For the majority of the pellets and the miniature bricks, the initial forming water content was 10 %. Based
on the results of the liquid limit for MS from Sisimiut, the upper limit for the forming water content was
wL ∼17 −20 %, since a higher amount of added water would result in a material in its liquid stage. Two
forming water contents of pellets (Pellet production 1), 10 −15 %, were investigated, and no signiﬁcant
difference was found in the results of water absorption and open porosity. The bulk density varied more for
pellets with a forming water content of 15 % (2036 −2145 kg/m3), than one of 10 % (2060 −2142 kg/m3),
==PAGE:88==
76
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION - FIRED BRICK PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
together with a more consistent increase of bulk density with increasing ﬁring temperature. See Fig. 9.8.
Furthermore, a larger linear drying shrinkage was observed. The drying shrinkage for pellets with a forming
water content of 10 % was 0.0 −0.2 %, whereas it was 0.2 −0.7 % for a forming water content of 15 %.
When forming the pellets by pressing, a larger amount of excess water was observed for pellets with a forming
water content of 15 %. The ﬁring shrinkage was similar for pellets containing 10 % and 15 % of forming
water. Hence the free water was assumed to have evaporated during drying. This indicates that the forming
water content, when using the dry-press method, shall be kept at low as possible, due to the lower shrinkage
and similar durability properties such as water absorption, open porosity and to a certain level, bulk density.
Mezencevova et al. (2012) investigated bricks with an addition of river sediments. Due to a high plastic limit
of both the clay and the river sediments, a high water content of 32−47 % was required for forming the bricks.
It was observed that, despite the slow drying process, shrinkage cracks were formed in bricks prepared from
mixes with higher water content, whereas no cracks from drying were observed in brick specimens with the
lower water content. No cracks were observed in miniature bricks nor in brick pellets in this project, and was
considered to be due to the low forming water content of 10 %.
Regarding the forming water content in bricks by Chen et al. (2011), the compressive strength increased up to
15 %, where the optimal value was found, whereupon the compressive strength decreased. Furthermore the
water absorption increased from ∼19.5 to 21.5 %, for forming water content ranging from 5 to 20 %.
11.3.5
Durability - Inﬂuence of additional material
An additional content of ﬁne grained residue from crushing of granitic rocks (CR) was used in the production
of brick pellets and miniature bricks. The main purpose was to lower the linear total shrinkage of the ﬁred
units, since Belmonte (2014) found that CR reduced shrinkage, whereas it unfortunately caused an increase in
porosity and water absorption, and a decrease bulk density. An unsorted fraction of CR was added to the bricks
made by Belmonte (2014).
The major element distribution of MS from Illulisat was, by an XRF-analysis, determined by Belmonte (2014),
and not of MS from Sisimiut. Due to their very comparable mineralogy, see Table 10.1, the major element
distributions were also considered comparable. Moreover, Belmonte (2014) determined the major element
distribution of the unsorted fraction of CR.
It is well known, that ﬂuxing agents as K and Na (and partly Mg and Ca) promote vitriﬁcation of the material
and thereby lowers the sintering temperature (Andersen et al., 1989; Holmboe, 2001). The content of ﬂuxing
agents, presented as oxides from an XRF-analysis, were almost identical for MS from Illulisat and for CR,
K2O = 3.3/3.1 wt% and Na2O = 2.8/2.5 wt%, respectively for MS and CR (Belmonte, 2014).
Based on the very similar ﬂuxing agents in MS and CR, it was not expected that CR would enhance vitriﬁcation,
which nevertheless was the scenario in the production of brick pellets. It was clearly observed for brick pellets
made of 80 % MS and 20 % CR, that they were initially beginning to produce melt when ﬁred at 1050 ◦C,
which was not the case for pellets made of 100 % MS, ﬁred the same temperature. See Fig. 9.1 for a photo of
pellets containing CR ﬁred at 1050 ◦C. This occurrence was not observed by Belmonte (2014).
This observation led to the question about of whether the major element distribution of the sorted and unsorted
fraction CR are similar. From the bulk mineralogical XRD-analysis by Belmonte (2014), the peak at d = 10
˚A represents mica (biotite and illite), and has a high intensity in the diffractogram for CR compared to MS.
According to Belmonte (2014), CR furthermore consist of a high percentage of quartz, amphibole, and feldspar.
The hardness of the minerals, given by Mohs scale (Hefferan and O’Brien, 2010; Geology.com, 2015), is 7 for
quartz, 6-6.5 for plagioclase (Na-, and Ca-feldspar), 5-6 for amphibole, 2.5-3 for biotite, and 1-2 for illite.
Due to these values it is reasonable to assume that the micas will break down before the harder minerals, and
therefore representing a higher in the ﬁner fraction of CR.
In this project, CR was sieved in order to investigate the effect of a ﬁner fraction (0.125 −2.0 mm). Since
micas contain potassium, K, and quarts only consists of SiO2, it was assessed that the ﬁner fraction had a
==PAGE:89==
11.4. SINTERING OF FIRED UNITS
77
higher content of ﬂuxing agents. Based on the above-mentioned assumptions, the improved water absorption
properties were considered to be due to the enhanced sintering of the material because of the higher content of
ﬂuxing agents in the ﬁner fraction of CR.
The water absorption of miniature bricks (after 24 h in cold water and 5 h of boiling) decreased from 14.1−11.8
% with a CR addition from 0 −20 %. See Table 9.5. The shrinkage furthermore decreased (from 0.7 −0.0 %),
but was already very low due to the high forming pressure.
The compressive strength was slightly lower for miniature bricks containing CR. 46.3 MPa without CR, and
40.6 MPa with CR.
Investigations of bricks with granite used as additional material were made by Vieira et al. (2004); Acchar
et al. (2006); Segadaes et al. (2005); Torres et al. (2009); Menezes et al. (2005), and showed very different
effects of the granite addition on properties such as shrinkage, water absorption, open porosity, bulk density
and compressive strength.
Vieira et al. (2004) investigated the addition of ﬁne grained granite powder waste in red ceramic units. The
supplementation caused an increase in shrinkage and bulk density, and a decrease in water absorption (23.7 −
18.9 %) with increasing content of granite waste (0 −40 %). It was observed that the granite waste had a
signiﬁcant effect on the extrusion behaviour. Furthermore an increase in granite waste decreased the drying
shrinkage, which is preferable in order to reduce the risk of appearance of cracks and dimensional defects in
ceramic pieces. The improved properties weres assessed to be due to the formation of a larger amount of liquid
phase during ﬁring, which contributes to reduced porosity. This was enhanced by the high content of ﬂuxing
material as K2O (5.2 wt%), and Na2O (2.9 wt%).
Segadaes et al. (2005) concluded that a content of 30 % of granite and marble waste could be added to the clay
mixture (for a production of ﬂoor tiles), without worsening the properties much, and outlined the possibilities
for ﬁring at lower temperatures when the granite waste was added, due to the higher content of ﬂuxing agents.
Acchar et al. (2006); Torres et al. (2009); Menezes et al. (2005) found small improving effect of adding granite
waste to the brick clay mixture up to 30−50 %, and concluded as well, that the addition of granite waste could
be successfully incorporated into the clay mixture.
Another aspect to consider would be that CR is a non-plastic material, which means that it lowers the plasticity
index of the material. This might induce some difﬁculties when forming the bricks. The clay mixture used in
the brick production by Vieira et al. (2004) had a plastic limit of wP = 35 % which is too high for the extrusion
method. The addition of granite waste lowered the plasticity index, and had therefore a signiﬁcant improving
effect on the extrusion behaviour.
When the dry-pressing method is used, the plasticity index is almost insigniﬁcant, whereas if the stiff-mud
method (mechanical extrusion) should be tried out, a water content of 10 −15 % together with a higher
plasticity index is required (Brick Industry Association, 2006b).
11.4
Sintering of ﬁred units
The sintering stage of the ﬁred units was analysed by a thermo-dilatometric analysis, and visually analysed on
SEM images of the dried and ﬁred units.
The following observations related to sintering of the ﬁred units were made.
• By the thermo-dilatometric analysis, it was obvious that the ﬁnal dilation was much lower for pellets
with a content of CR, than for pellets only containing MS, both types were ﬁred at 1025 ◦C. The ﬁnal
dilation of pellets, with an addition of 20 % of CR, experienced an expansion of ∼4 %. The ﬁnal dilation
of pellets of 100 % of MS, experienced a shrinkage that was very much dependent on the maximum ﬁring
temperature. When ﬁred at 1025 ◦C, the shrinkage was ∼1.7 %, whereas it was much higher, ∼5.1 %,
when ﬁred at 1050 ◦C.
==PAGE:90==
78
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION - FIRED BRICK PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
• By the visual analysis of SEM images, it was difﬁcult to observe a large difference between a pellet of
100 % MS, that was dried (105 ◦C), and ﬁred (1050 ◦C). See Fig. 9.9. On the contrary, a large difference
was to observe between a pellet of 80 % MS, and 20 % CR, that was dried (105 ◦C), and ﬁred (1050 ◦C).
See Fig. 9.10.
• The difference in dilation and micro structure between a uniform ﬁring cycle of ∼5 ◦C/min, and a ﬁring
cycle lowered to ∼2 ◦C/min around the quarts inversion, was not observed to be very large.
11.4.1
Sintering - Thermal dilation during ﬁring
The ﬁring behaviour of the units, with and without an addition of CR, ﬁred at two different ﬁring cycles are
shown in Fig. 9.11, Fig. 9.12, and Fig. 9.13. The ﬁring cycles were lowered (from 5 −2 ◦C/min) in the range
of 520 −620 ◦C around the quarts inversion 573 ◦C. The effect was a slightly smaller thermal dilation around
this temperature, see Table 9.3.
When analysing the ﬁring behaviour in Fig. 9.13, the following observation were made and will be discussed
latter in this section.
• An abrupt dilatometric change in volume was present for all types of pellets around the quarts inversion
(573 ◦C). The change in volume was slightly lowered, but still valid for pellets ﬁred with a lowered ﬁring
cycle in the temperature range of 520 −620 ◦C.
• The dilation curves for all types of pellets and ﬁring cycles, were very similar until ∼730 ◦C. After
∼730 ◦C the dilation of pellets of 100 % of MS continued increasing with the same rate as from after
the quarts inversion. Pellets containing 20 % of CR experienced a raise in the dilatometric increasing
rate.
• The increase in the dilation rate after ∼730 ◦C for pellets containing 20 % of CR, was assessed to be
due to the presumably higher content of ﬂuxing agents in the ﬁne fraction of CR, which enhance the
sintering process, as described in Section 11.3.5.
• The maximum dilation of all types of pellets was found to be around 900 ◦C.
• The higher shrinkage developed during ﬁring at 1050 ◦C compared to that of 1025 ◦C, was assessed to
be due to a higher degree of sintering.
• The smaller shrinkage developed during ﬁring at the maximum temperature (1025 ◦C) for pellets con-
taining 20 % of CR, was assessed to be due to the higher content of quarts and other minerals less likely
to produce melt.
Dilation around quartz inversion, 573 ◦C
No great improvement of pellets ﬁred with a lowered heating and cooling rate around the quarts inversion. For
pellets of 100 % MS, the abrupt expansion at 573 ◦C during heating, was slightly lowered from ∼1.5/1.0−0.8
% (Test 1,2 and 4). For pellets with an addition of 20 % CR, the abrupt expansion at 573 ◦C during heating,
was lowered from ∼1.5 −0.8 % (Test 3 and 5). The lowered heating-/cooling rate in the range around the
quarts inversion did not have a high inﬂuence on the dilatometric change in volume. The same tendencies were
observed for all pellets during cooling, but in a lower scale.
==PAGE:91==
11.4. SINTERING OF FIRED UNITS
79
Increase in expansion for CR, ∼730 ◦C
Around ∼730 ◦C the effect of the added granite (CR) was beginning to be visible, since the expansion of the
pellet with CR increased in proportion to the sample without CR.
It was considered that the additional expansion was a result of the higher content of ﬂuxing agents as described
in Section 11.3.5. Furthermore gasses from decomposition of e.g. illite, entrapped inside the closed pores in
the matrix, might be due to the expansion.
Maximum dilation, ∼900 ◦C
The uniform expansion of both the types of pellets with and without CR occurs up to ∼900 ◦C, whereupon
the sintering of the pellets begins. The same behaviour was observed by Segadaes et al. (2005); Djangang et al.
(2008); Pontikes et al. (2007). According to Acchar et al. (2009) the collapse of the structure of clay matrix
occurs due to the dehydroxylation of the clay minerals above 900 ◦C. Schomburg and Zwahr (1997) states that
the dehydroxylation of biotite occurs at 980 −1150 ◦C, and Belmonte (2014) found that mica phases were
decomposed at temperatures below 1000 ◦C.
11.4.2
Sintering - Micro structure of sintered samples
The micro structure was analysed on SEM images of the fracture surface of ﬁred and dried brick pellets. In
Fig. 9.10 and Fig. 9.9 images of pellets with and without CR are illustrated. The difference between the dried
and ﬁred pellet only containing MS was difﬁcult to see, whereas a larger difference was observed between
the dried and ﬁred pellet with an addition of CR. The almost invisible difference between dried and ﬁred
(1050 ◦C) pellet in Fig. 9.9 was considered to be very surprising, since the durability properties were improved
signiﬁcantly for pellets ﬁred at 1050 ◦C compared to pellets ﬁred at 1000 ◦C.
The development of sintered elements in ﬁred units of contaminated marine sediments was investigated by
Romero et al. (2008, 2009) and reservoir sediments by Chiang et al. (2008). SEM images of the fracture surface
of specimen ﬁred at different temperatures (1000 −1200 ◦C) revealed that there was a clear development of
the micro structure with increasing temperature, even for relatively small changes in temperature.
It would be interesting to investigate the sintering behaviour and development in micro structure of pellets of
MS from Sisimiut ﬁred at higher temperatures, and to determine at what temperature a visible change in the
material occurs.
Segadaes et al. (2005) investigated clay products with an addition of marble and granite rejects, with a content
of ﬂuxing agents (K2O, Na2O) which was higher than in the replaced clay. On SEM images with and without
the granite reject, it was evident that the ﬁred units containing granite waste showed clear signs of vitriﬁcation
and a higher degree of closed porosity.
According to Acchar et al. (2006), a sign of an initial sintering process is that particles are bonded (grain
growth). The presence of a liquid phase in the ﬁred material is characterized by rounded, smooth surfaces,
and at the last level before vitriﬁcation, glassy bonds between grains, well-deﬁned grain boundaries, and many
isolated rounded pores are distinct.
==PAGE:92==
80
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION - FIRED BRICK PELLETS AND MINIATURE BRICKS
==PAGE:93==
12. Conclusion
The possibility of making bricks using ﬁne grained marine sediments from Sisimiut, Greenland, as primary ma-
terial, was investigated in this study. Fired units (pellets and miniature bricks) of ﬁne grained marine sediments
were produced and their durability properties and ﬁring behaviour characterized. Additionally, investigations
of the geotechnical, chemical and mineralogical properties were carried out.
From the experimental work performed in this study, the following conclusions are derived:
• The geotechnical investigations revealed a ﬁne grained silty clay loam of low plasticity, with an even
grain size distribution. All samples were found to have a low loss on ignition, low content of sulphur,
carbon, calcium carbonate and sulphate, but a high concentration of chlorine, which might be problem-
atic when used in a production of building bricks. The mineralogical composition of the material showed
a dominance of feldspar, and a low content of clay minerals, which correlates with the low plasticity.
• A preliminary production of ﬁred brick pellets was performed in order to evaluate the inﬂuence of differ-
ent variables in the production procedure and composition of raw materials. It was found, that increasing
ﬁring temperature, forming pressure, and addition of granite waste improved the quality and durability of
the ﬁred pellets. On the other hand, an increase in ﬁring time did not lead to any signiﬁcant improvements
of the properties. Additionally, the content of CR decreased the linear ﬁring shrinkage.
• Firing behaviour of samples of ﬁne grained marine sediments conﬁrmed that an addition of granite waste
lowered the linear shrinkage substantially.
• The pellets and miniature bricks, when ﬁred, obtained a reddish colour, which was magniﬁed with in-
creasing ﬁring temperature. The reddish colour corresponds with the low content of carbon. No surface
cracking was observed, which was connected with the low content of initial forming water and the dry-
pressing forming method.
• Physical and mechanical properties of miniature bricks were obtained by following ASTM-C62 (2013)
in order to fulﬁl the requirements for building bricks exposed to severe weathering. The compressive
strength (40 −46 MPa) and water absorption after boiling for 5 h (12 −14 %), met the requirements for
the SW grade, though the saturation coefﬁcient (0.90 −0.92 %), and thereby the water absorption after
submersion in cold water for 24 h (11 −13 %), was too high for meeting the SW grade by ASTM-C62
(2013). The recommended improvement would be to increase the ﬁring temperature.
Based on the obtained results, it is demonstrated that ﬁne grained marine sediments as the primary material
in the production of ﬁred bricks is promising, even though all the requirements by ASTM-C62 (2013) were not
successfully fulﬁlled.
12.1
Recommendation for future studies
The recommendations for future studies are divided into two. One relates to the optimization of the ﬁred prod-
uct and the other to obtaining a better understanding of the raw material. The objective for future studies will
be to produce a brick with the required properties for severe weathering according to ASTM-C62 (2013).
81
==PAGE:94==
82
CHAPTER 12. CONCLUSION
Future studies related to obtain a better understanding of the raw material:
• Determination of the major element distribution of MS from Sisimiut in order to compare it with other
marine sediments from Greenland. Moreover, a determination of the major element distribution of the
ﬁner fraction of CR, since it would be interesting to analyse whether the content of ﬂuxing agents is
higher in the ﬁner, sorted fraction.
• Determination of the plasticity properties of MS mixed with an addition of CR.
• A more thorough investigation of the chlorine concentration in the area around Sisimiut since differences
were observed between this study and the study by Olsen and Johansen (2013).
Future studies related to optimization of the ﬁred product:
• Firing of units with MS at higher temperatures to investigate the effect of the ﬁring temperature and to
determine the temperature, at which a visual change in the micro structure occurs.
• The addition of an even ﬁner fraction of CR, since Vieira et al. (2004) found that an addition of very ﬁne
fraction of granite waste improved properties such as water absorption.
• Different forming pressures, and -methods such as mechanical extrusion. Investigations of the inﬂuence
of a larger addition of forming water, which is required if e.g. mechanical extrusion shall be used for
forming the bricks.
